Shirkah — Partnership
beginner
8 min
Definition and Types of Partnership (Shirkah)
Definition of partnership with types, pillars, and evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define shirkah linguistically and legally, distinguish between shirkat al-milk and shirkat al-aqd, and state the rukn and evidence for each.

Core Idea
Partnership in Hanafi fiqh includes both co-ownership and contract; each has distinct pillars and rulings.

This lesson opens the Book of Partnership. We examine the Hanafi definition of shirkah — both its linguistic root of mixing and its legal application as co-ownership and contractual partnership. The text establishes that partnership is not limited to formal contracts; even inheritance creates a form of partnership. We will unpack the key concepts, pillars, and the Quranic and Prophetic evidence that legitimizes these transactions.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic from legal definition
The word shirkah literally means mixing, but in law it refers to shared ownership or a contractual arrangement. Identify the difference.
The linguistic meaning helps understand the underlying concept of mixing shares.
2
Identify the two types
From the text, list shirkat al-milk (co-ownership) and shirkat al-aqd (contractual partnership). Understand that the first arises from inheritance or purchase, the second from mutual agreement.
The matn gives inheritance and purchase as examples, not restrictions.
3
Determine the rukn for each type
For co-ownership, the rukn is physical mixing (ikhtilat); for contract, it is verbal expression (lafz). Why is mixing necessary for co-ownership?
The rukn differs, so confusing them leads to incorrect rulings.
4
Examine the evidence
The Quran verse 'they are partners in the third' (4:12) and the hadith of the Prophet's partnership with al-Sa'ib in Sunan Abi Dawud. How do these proofs support the permissibility?
The Quran proof is specific to co-ownership; the Sunnah proof is general.
5
Apply the condition
All parties must be legally qualified (qabilun lil-shirkah). What does this mean? It includes being of sound mind and age, and having legal capacity.
This condition ensures valid consent and capability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشركة
۲۷۹
كتاب الشركة
شركة الملك أن يملك اثنان عيناً إرثاً أو شراء وكل أجنبي في قسط صاحبه
كتاب الشركة
أولاها للمفقود لتناسبهما بوجهين كون مال أحدهما أمانة في يد الآخر كما أن مال المفقود أمانة في يد الحاضر، وكون الاشتراك قد يتحقق في مال المفقود كما لو مات مورثه وله وارث آخر والمفقود حي. والشركة لغة خلط النصيبين بحيث لا يتميز أحدهما، وما قيل إنه اختلاط النصيبين تساهل فإن الشركة اسم المصدر والمصدر الشرك مصدر شركت الرجل أشركه شركاً فظهر أنها فعل الإنسان وفعله الخلط، وأما الاختلاط فصفة للمال تثبت عن فعلهما ليس لها اسم من المادة ولا يظن أن اسمه الاشتراك لأن الاشتراك فعلهما أيضاً مصدر اشترك الرجلان افتعال من الشركة؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وذكر أنها بإسكان الراء في المعروف وسكت عن الأول. وفي القاموس : الشرك والشركة بكسرهما وضم الثاني بمعنى. وقد اشتركا وشارك أحدهما الآخر. والشرك بالكسر وكأمير المشارك والجمع أشراك وشركاء اهـ. وفي التبيين : إطلاق الشركة على العقد مجاز لكونه سبباً له. وفي فتح القدير: وركنها في شركة العين اختلاطهما، وفي شركة العقد اللفظ المفيد له ويقال الشركة على العقد نفسه، فإذا قيل شركة العقد بالإضافة فهي إضافة بيانية وشرعيتها بالكتاب والسنة والمعقول؛ أما الكتاب فقوله تعالى فهم شركاء في الثلث [النساء : ١٢] وهو خاص بشركة العين. وأما السنة فما في سنن أبي داود عن السائب أنه قال للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : كنت شريكي في الجاهلية كما في فتح القدير. وفي المحيط : شرط جوازها كون الواحد قابلاً للشركة وحكمها في شركة الملك صيرورة المجتمع من النصيبين مشتركاً بينهما وفي شركة العقد صيرورة المعقود عليه أو ما يستفاد به مشتركاً بينهما قوله : شركة الملك أن يملك اثنان عينا إرثاً أو شراء) بيان للنوع الأول منها، وقوله إرثاً أو شراء» مثال لا قيد فلا يرد أن ظاهره القصر عليهما أنه لا
مع
كتاب الشركة
قوله: (وتمامه في جامع الفصولين (الخ) أقول : أوضحه في جامع الفصولين من الخامس
Book of Partnership. 279. Book of Partnership. Partnership of ownership is that two persons own an asset by inheritance or purchase, and each is a stranger regarding the share of the other. Book of Partnership. The first (type) is connected to the missing person because of two similarities: that the property of one is a trust in the hand of the other, just as the property of the missing person is a trust in the hand of the present one, and that partnership may occur in the property of the missing person, as if his ancestor dies and he has another heir and the missing person is alive. Partnership linguistically is the mixing of two shares such that neither is distinguishable. What is said that it is the mixing of two shares is lenient, for partnership is a verbal noun, and the source is 'shirk' from 'sharaktu al-rajul ashrakahu shirkan'. Thus it appears that it is an action of the human, and his action is to mix. As for mixing, it is a quality of the property that results from their action; there is no name from the root for it, and one should not think that its name is 'ishtirak' because 'ishtirak' is also their action, a masdar from ishtaraka, an ifti'al from shirkah. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. It is mentioned that it is with sukun of the ra' in the known, and he was silent about the first. In al-Qamus: al-shirk and al-shirkah (with kasra of both or damm of the second) have the same meaning. They share and one partners the other. Al-shirk (with kasra) and like amir meaning partner, plural ashrak and shuraka'. End quote. In al-Tabyin: Applying partnership to the contract is metaphorical because it is a cause for it. In Fath al-Qadir: Its rukn in co-ownership partnership is the mixing of the two, and in contractual partnership it is the expression conveying the contract. Partnership is spoken of regarding the contract itself, so when 'shirkat al-aqd' is said with idafah, it is an explanatory idafah. Its legitimacy is established by the Book, the Sunnah, and reason. As for the Book, His statement (ta'ala): 'they are partners in the third' (4:12), which is specific to co-ownership. As for the Sunnah, that which is in Sunan Abi Dawud from al-Sa'ib that he said to the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wasallam): 'You were my partner in the Jahiliyyah' as in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Muhit: The condition of its permissibility is that each one is capable of partnership. Its ruling in co-ownership partnership is that the combined property from the two shares becomes shared between them, and in contractual partnership it is that the contracted object or what is acquired by it becomes shared between them. His statement: 'Partnership of ownership is that two persons own an asset by inheritance or purchase' is an explanation of the first type. His statement 'by inheritance or purchase' is an example, not a restriction, so it is not objected that its apparent meaning restricts to these two, because no... With the book of partnership. His statement: 'And its completion is in Jami' al-Fusulayn' ... I say: It is explained in Jami' al-Fusulayn in the fifth [section].
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a definition_ruling from the sharh beginning the Book of Partnership (Kitab al-Shirkah). It defines shirkah linguistically as the mixing of shares, and legally as co-ownership of an asset (shirkat al-milk) or contractual partnership (shirkat al-'aqd). The matn states the first type: co-ownership arises through inheritance or purchase, and each co-owner is a stranger to the other's share. The sharh explains that this is not restrictive but exemplary. The legitimacy of partnership is proven by the Quran (Surah al-Nisa, verse 12, referring to co-ownership) and the Sunnah (the hadith of al-Sa'ib where the Prophet was his partner in pre-Islamic times). The rukn (pillar) for co-ownership is the mixing of properties, and for contractual partnership it is the verbal expression. A condition for permissibility is that each party is legally qualified for partnership. The ruling (hukm) for co-ownership is that the combined properties become jointly owned, while for contractual partnership, the object of the contract or its profit is jointly owned. The passage also notes linguistic points about the pronunciation and derivation of the term.

Key Terms
شركة الملك (shirkat al-milk): Co-ownership of an asset arising from inheritance or purchase without a partnership contract.
شركة العقد (shirkat al-'aqd): Contractual partnership formed by mutual offer and acceptance to share capital and profit.
ركن (rukn): The integral element without which the contract or ownership is not valid: mixing for co-ownership, utterance for contractual.
اختلاط (ikhtilat): The physical mixing of properties such that they become indistinguishable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the rukn (pillar) of contractual partnership (shirkat al-'aqd) according to the passage?
A) Mixing of properties
B) A clear verbal expression
C) Registration with authorities
D) Both parties being merchants

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law recognizes two forms of partnership: co-ownership (shirkat al-milk) and contractual (shirkat al-aqd), each with its own pillar and evidence.

Types of Partnership: Compulsory and Optional
Defines types of partnership (jabriyya/ikhtiyariyya).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between compulsory and optional partnership and classify given scenarios accordingly.

Core Idea
Partnership is either inherited (jabri) or agreed (ikhtiyari); each has distinct legal origins.

This lesson unpacks the foundational classification of partnership in Hanafi fiqh. The source text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that partnership (shirkah) is of two types: compulsory (jabriyyah), which arises without choice from inheritance, and optional (ikhtiyariyyah), which arises from mutual agreement such as purchase or gift. Understanding this distinction is crucial because the legal rules governing each type differ significantly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the passage and note the two categories: compulsory and optional. Compulsory partnership is forced by inheritance; optional partnership is chosen by the partners.
The text uses 'jabriyya' for compulsory and 'ikhtiyariyya' for optional.
2
Classify examples
Determine which type applies to given scenarios: inheritance (compulsory), joint purchase (optional), gift (optional).
Mixing of assets without mutual action may be treated differently.
3
Recognize the scope of each type
Understand that compulsory partnership only covers inherited assets; optional partnership extends to any agreed property, including debts according to the relied-upon opinion.
The text then moves to the special case of debt partnership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشركة
يتعسر مع يمنع يوصي يقتصر عليهما بل تكون فيما إذا ملكاها هبة أو صدقة أو استيلاء بأن استوليا على مال حربي أو اختلاطاً كما إذا اختلط مالهما من غير صنع من أحدهما أو اختلط بخلطهما خلطاً . التمييز أو كالحنطة الشعير. والحاصل أنها نوعان جبرية واختيارية، فأشار إلى الجبرية بالإرث وإلى الاختيارية بالشراء كما في المحيط. وذكر أن من الاختيارية أن . لهما بمال فيقبلان وظاهر قولهم عيناً يدل على إخراج الدين فقيل إن الشركة فيه مجاز لأنه وصف شرعي لا يملك، وقد يقال بل يملك شرعاً وقد جازت هبته ممن عليه الدين ودفع بأنها مجاز عن الإسقاط ولذا لم تجز من غير من عليه الدين وفي فتح القدير : والحق ما ذكروا ملكه ولذا ملك ما عنه من العين على الاشتراك حتى إذا دفع من عليه الدين إلى أحدهما كان للآخر الرجوع عليه بنصف ما أخذ وليس له أن يقول هذا الذي أخذته حصتي وما بقي على المديون حصتك، ولا يصح من المديون أيضاً أن يعطيه شيئاً على أنه قضاه وأخر الآخر، وسيأتي في الصلح أن بما أخذ من الحيلة في اختصاص دون شريكه أن يهبه من عليه مقدار حصته ويبرئه هو من حصته فلو قال المصنف أن يملك متعدد عيناً أو ديناً لكان أولى.
Book of Partnership. It is difficult with [؟] prohibits, bequeaths, restricts to them both, but it is when they own it by gift or charity or seizure such as seizing war booty, or mixing such as when their property mixes without the action of either, or mixes by their joint mixing. Distinguishing or like wheat and barley. The summary is that it is of two types: compulsory and optional. He indicated compulsory by inheritance and optional by purchase as in al-Muhit. He mentioned that from optional is that ... they have wealth and they accept. The apparent meaning of their saying 'ayn' indicates the exclusion of debt. So it was said that partnership in it is metaphorical because it is a legal description that cannot be owned. And it may be said rather it is owned legally, and its gift has been permitted from the one who owes the debt. And it was rebutted that it is metaphorical for cancellation, and therefore it is not permitted from other than the debtor. And in Fath al-Qadir: The truth is what they mentioned: his ownership, and therefore he owns what he receives of the 'ayn on joint basis, so that when the debtor pays one of them, the other has recourse against him for half of what he took. And he cannot say: 'This that I took is my share, and what remains on the debtor is your share.' Nor is it valid for the debtor to give him something as payment for him and postpone the other. And it will come in the chapter of sulh that among the tricks for allocation without his partner is that the debtor gifts him the amount of his share and he discharges him from his share. So if the author had said 'that multiple people own an 'ayn or a debt' it would have been better.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines the two types of partnership: compulsory (jabriyyah) and optional (ikhtiyariyyah). Compulsory partnership occurs through inheritance, while optional arises from purchase or other means like gift. The main discussion here is whether partnership can exist in debts (dayn). Some scholars say it is metaphorical because debt is not physically owned, but the Hanafi position as stated by al-Kasani in Fath al-Qadir is that debt is indeed owned legally, hence partnership in it is valid. Evidence: if the debtor pays one partner, the other can claim half. This confirms mutual ownership. The author, al-Marghinani (or the commentator) suggests that the matn author could have explicitly included debt in the definition of partnership to avoid confusion.

Key Terms
شركة جبرية (shirkah jabriyyah): Compulsory partnership arising from inheritance without choice.
شركة اختيارية (shirkah ikhtiyariyyah): Optional partnership through purchase, gift, or other mutual agreement.
دين (dayn): A debt or financial obligation, which Hanafi fiqh considers legally ownable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, why is partnership in debt considered valid?
A) Because debt is a legal description that cannot be owned.
B) Because the debtor can gift the debt, and one partner can recover from the other.
C) Because it is a form of compulsory partnership.
D) Because all scholars agree on its validity.

Key Takeaway
Partnership is either compulsory (inheritance) or optional (agreement); debt is ownable, so partnership in debt is valid in Hanafi fiqh.

Selling a Partner's Share: Permission Required in Mixing Partnerships
Ruling on sale of share with distinction between two partnership types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between inception partnership (shirkat al-in‘iqād) and mixing partnership (shirkat al-khalṭ) and state the ruling on selling one's share to an outsider in each case.

Core Idea
In mixing partnership, selling to an outsider needs permission; in inception partnership, it does not.

A common question in partnership law is whether a partner can sell his share without the other's consent. The answer depends on how the partnership was formed. The Hanafi school draws a critical distinction between property that is co-owned from the start and property that becomes mixed later. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of shirkah mentioned
The passage contrasts partnership formed from inception (both buying or inheriting together) with partnership by mixing (khalṭ or ikhtilāṭ). In the first, every grain is jointly owned; in the second, each grain is individually owned.
Inception = original joint ownership; mixing = later physical blending of separate properties.
2
Determine the ruling for sale to an outsider
In inception partnership, a partner may sell his undivided share to an outsider without permission because the share is conceptually separable. In mixing partnership, he may not sell to an outsider without the partner's permission because delivery would necessarily involve the partner's property.
Sale to the partner himself is always allowed in both types.
3
Understand the ratio legis (ʿillah)
The key difference is the nature of ownership. In inception, each particle is co‑owned; so selling a share transfers a fraction of each particle. In mixing, each particle is wholly owned by one party; selling a share means transferring specific grains that are physically intermingled, making delivery impossible without the partner's consent.
4
Apply to a hypothetical scenario
Consider two persons, A and B, who each bring a sack of wheat and mix them into one pile. A wants to sell his share of the mixed wheat to a third party C. According to the Hanafi ruling, A cannot sell to C without B's permission, because A's wheat cannot be separated from B's without mixing.
If A and B had jointly purchased the wheat together, they could sell their shares without each other's permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وكل أجنبي في قسط صاحبه أي وكل واحد من الشريكين ممنوع من التصرف في نصيب صاحبه لغير الشريك إلا بإذنه لعدم تضمنها الوكالة والقسط بالكسر الحصة والنصيب؛ كذا في القاموس. ولم يذكر المصنف حكم بيع أحدهما حصته وحكم الانتفاع بها أما الأول فقالوا : يجوز بيع أحدهما نصيبه من شريكه في جميع الصور ومن غير شريكه بغير إذنه إلا في صورة الخلط والاختلاط فإنه لا يجوز إلا بإذنه. والفرق أن الشركة إذا كانت بينهما من الابتداء بأن اشتريا حنطة أو ورثاها كانت كل حبة مشتركة بينهما فبيع كل منهما نصيبه شائعاً جائز من الشريك والأجنبي بخلاف ما إذا كانت بالخلط أو الاختلاط كان كل حبة مملوكة بجميع أجزائها ليس للآخر فيها شركة، فإذا باع نصيبه من غير الشريك لا يقدر على تسليمه إلا مخلوطاً بنصيب الشريك فيتوقف على إذنه بخلاف بيعه من الشريك
His saying: 'And every foreigner in the portion of his companion' means each of the two partners is prohibited from disposing in the share of his companion for other than the partner except with his permission because it does not include agency. Al-qist (portion) means share and portion; as in al-Qamus. And the author did not mention the ruling of one of them selling his share and the ruling of benefiting from it. As for the first, they said: It is permissible for one of them to sell his share to his partner in all cases, and to a non-partner without his permission except in the case of mixing and blending, for it is not permissible except with his permission. The difference is that if the partnership was between them from the beginning, such as both buying wheat or inheriting it, every grain is jointly owned between them, so selling each of them his share as undivided is permissible to the partner and the outsider. Contrary to if it was by mixing or blending, then every grain is owned in all its parts, the other has no share in it. So if he sells his share to a non-partner, he cannot deliver it except mixed with the partner's share, so it depends on his permission, contrary to selling to the partner.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage clarifies the restriction on partners disposing of each other's shares. The key distinction is between partnership from inception (e.g., buying jointly) where ownership is undivided from the start, and partnership by mixing where each grain is fully owned by one. In the first case, selling one's share to an outsider is fine because the share is conceptually separable even if physically mixed? Actually the text says the opposite: in inception partnership, every grain is jointly owned, so selling a share is possible. In mixing, each grain is individually owned, so selling one's share is impossible without permission because it is physically inseparable. The commentator notes that the author omitted rulings on sale and use; he then provides the ruling: sale to a partner is always allowed; sale to an outsider is allowed in inception partnership but requires permission in mixing partnership.

Key Terms
قسط (qist): Portion or share in a partnership property.
خلط (khalṭ): Physical mixing of separate properties, creating a mixture where each particle is individually owned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it necessary for a partner to get permission from the other partner before selling his share to an outsider?
A) When the partnership was formed by both purchasing the property together.
B) When the partnership was formed by mixing their separate properties.
C) Always, regardless of how the partnership was formed.
D) Never, each partner can sell his share freely.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on selling a partner's share hinges on whether the partnership originated as joint ownership or by subsequent mixing, with permission required only in the latter due to delivery constraints.

Dwelling in the Joint House in Absence: The Hanafi Istihsan Ruling
Expansion of rulings on joint property use in absence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a partner dwelling in the entire joint house when the other is absent, explain the istihsan basis, and distinguish the rulings for other types of joint property (servant, animal).

Core Idea
Istihsan allows a partner to dwell in the entire house and use the entire servant when the other is absent, but riding is not permitted due to potential harm.

Imagine you co-own a house with an absent partner. Can you live in the entire house? The Hanafi school answers with a nuanced distinction: by strict analogy (qiyas) you may only use your share, but by juristic preference (istihsan) you may use the whole for dwelling, because otherwise the property's benefit would be lost. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and the default qiyas ruling.
List the acts discussed: dwelling in the house, using the servant, riding the animal, cultivating land. Note that qiyas prohibits any use of the other partner's share without permission.
Qiyas says: disposing of another's property is haram.
2
Understand the istihsan exception for dwelling and servant.
The present partner may dwell in the entire house and use the entire servant because of custom (urf) and necessity (darurah), and it is considered as if each partner owns the whole for these purposes.
Istihsan overrides qiyas due to practical need.
3
Note the restriction on riding the animal.
Riding an animal is not permitted because people differ in riding styles and it may cause harm to the absent partner's share.
Harm (darar) is the differentiating factor.
4
Apply the principle to other properties (land).
The passage mentions a difference: some Hanafi scholars permit cultivation of the land to the extent of one's share, but not the whole. The preferred view (mufta bihi) allows the present partner to cultivate his share and also tend the vineyard, selling fruit and keeping his share.
Land cultivation requires more care than dwelling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثلاثين في التصرفات في الأعيان المشتركة فقال : أرض أو كرم بين حاضر وغائب أو بين بالغ ويتيم، فالحاضر أو البالغ يرفع الأمر إلى القاضي، ولو لم يرفع ففي الأرض يزرع بحصته ويطيب له ذلك ويقوم على الكرم فيبيع ثمره ويأخذ حصته ويوقف حصة الغائب ويبيع له ذلك، وإذا قدم الغائب ضمنه القيمة أو أجاز بيعه. وذكر في مواضع أخر عن محمد رحمه الله لو أخذ الشريك نصيبه من الثمن وأكله جاز ويبيع نصيب الغائب ويحفظ ثمنه، فلو حضر صاحبه يخير كما مر، فلو لم يحضر فهو كلقطة . قالت هذا استحسان وبه أخذ ولو أدى الخراج كان متبرعاً. وذكر محمد رحمه الله صل : غاب أحد شريكي الدار فأراد الحاضر أن يسكنها رجلاً أو يؤجرها لا ينبغي أن يفعل ذلك في ديانة إذ التصرف في ملك الغير حرام حقاً لله تعالى وللمالك، ولا يمنع منه قضاء لأن الإنسان لا يمنع عن التصرف فيما في يده لو لم ينازعه أحد، فلو أجر وأخذ الأجر يرد على شريكه نصيبه لو قدر وإلا تصدق به لتمكن الخبث فيه لحق شريكه فكان كغاصب أجر يتصدق بالأجر أو يرده على المالك. وأما نصيبه فيطيب له إذ لا خبث فيه هذا لو أسكن غيره، أما لو سكن بنفسه ليس له ذلك ديانة قياساً وله ذلك استحساناً إذ له أن يسكنها بلا إذن شريكه حال حضوره إذ يتعذر عليه الاستئذان في كل مرة على هذا أمر الدور فيما بين الناس فكان له أن يسكن حال غيبته بخلاف إسكان غيره إذ ليس له ذلك حال حضرته بلا إذنه فكذا حال غيبته (غن) دار بينهما غير مقسومة غاب أحدهما وسع الحاضر أن يسكن بقدر حصته فيسكن الدار كلها وكذا خادم بينهما غاب أحدهما فللحاضر أن يستخدمه بحصته، وفي الدابة لا يركبها الحاضر لتفاوت الناس في الركوب لا السكنى والاستخدام فيتضرر الغائب بركوبها لا بهمان عن محمد رحمه الله : للحاضر أن يسكن كل الدار لو خاف خرابها لو لم يسكنها. وعن ح رحمه الله : ليس للحاضر في الأرض أن يزرع بقدر نصيبه وفي الدار له أن يسكنها (بر) أن له ذلك في الوجهين فلو سكن الدار أحد شريكيهما بغيبة الآخر لا يلزمه الأجر ولو أعدت للاستغلال، والأصل أن الدار المشتركة في حق السكنى وتوابعه تجعل كملك لكل من الشريكين على الكمال إذ لو لم تجعل كذلك. يمتنع كل منهما من دخول وقعود ووضع أمتعة فيتعطل عليهما منافع ملكهما وهو لم يجز فصار الحاضر ساكناً في ملك نفسه فكيف يلزم الأجر؟ اهـ . وهذه المسائل كثيرة الوقوع فلتحفظ. وفي الظهيرية : ولو قال لآخر ما اشتريت اليوم من أنواع التجارات فهو بيني وبينك وقال الآخر نعم فهو جائز، وكذلك لو قال كل واحد منهما لصاحبه ذلك لأن هذه شركة في الشراء والشركة في الشراء جائزة، وليس لأحد منهما أن يبيع حصة الآخر مما اشترى إلا بإذن صاحبه لأنهما اشتركا في الشراء لا في البيع ، ولو اشترى رجل عبداً فقال له رجل اشركني
And the thirtieth [chapter] on dispositions in joint properties: He said: Land or vineyard between a present and absent, or between an adult and orphan. The present or adult raises the matter to the judge. If he does not raise it, then in land he cultivates his share and it is lawful for him, and he tends the vineyard, sells its fruit, takes his share, and stops the absent's share and sells it for him. When the absent returns, he is given the choice of guaranteeing the price or ratifying the sale. It is mentioned elsewhere from Muhammad رحمه الله: If the partner takes his share of the price and consumes it, it is permissible, and he sells the absent's share and keeps its price. When his partner arrives, he is given choice as before. If he does not arrive, it becomes like a luqatah. He said: This is istihsan and he adopted it. If he pays the land tax, he is a volunteer. Muhammad رحمه الله mentioned: If one partner of a house is absent and the present wishes to house a person or lease it, he should not do so in religious terms (diyanatan) because disposing of another's property is haram as a right of Allah and the owner. But the qadi does not prevent him because a person is not prevented from disposing of what is in his hand if no one contests. If he leases and takes rent, he returns the partner's share if possible, otherwise gives it as charity because of the impurity (khabath) due to the partner's right. He is like a usurper who leases and gives the rent as charity or returns it to the owner. As for his own share, it is lawful for him because there is no impurity. This if he houses someone else. As for if he dwells himself, he does not have that diyanatan by qiyas, but he has it by istihsan because he may dwell without his partner's permission when present since it would be difficult to ask permission every time. This is the practice among people. So he may dwell during absence, unlike housing others because he cannot do that when present without permission, so similarly during absence. For an undivided house, if one is absent, the present may dwell in his share and thus the entire house. Likewise, a servant between them, the absent one may use his share. As for an animal, the present may not ride it because people vary in riding, unlike dwelling and using, so the absent would be harmed by riding but not by the others. From Muhammad: The present may dwell in the entire house if he fears its ruin if not occupied. From H.: The present may not cultivate in the land to the extent of his share, but in the house he may dwell. He said: He has that in both cases. So if one of two partners dwells in the house without the other, he is not liable for rent even if it was prepared for investment. The principle is that a jointly owned house regarding dwelling and its accessories is made like complete ownership for each partner, because if not, each would be prevented from entering, sitting, placing luggage, and the benefits would be disrupted, which is not permissible. So the present dwells in his own property, so how can rent be due? End. These issues occur frequently, so memorize. In al-Zahiriyyah: If one says to another, 'Whatever I buy today of types of trade is between me and you,' and the other says yes, it is valid. Similarly, if each says that to the other, because this is a partnership in purchase, which is valid. Neither may sell the other's share of what was purchased without the other's permission because they partnered in purchase, not in sale. And if a man bought a slave and another said, 'Include me,' ...
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage covers the extensive fiqh of using joint property when one partner is absent. The general rule is that each partner has the right to use his own share, but in practice, entire use may be necessary. The Hanafi school, with istihsan, permits a partner to dwell in the entire house, use the entire servant, but not ride a riding animal due to potential harm. Cultivation of land is permitted if it benefits the land. If the present partner takes rent, he must segregate the absent's share. The 'asad' (principle) is that for purposes like dwelling, the property is considered as if each owns the whole, preventing disruption. The latter part from al-Zahiriyyah addresses partnership in purchase, where the agreement covers purchase only, so sale requires both consents.

Key Terms
ديانة (diyanatan): In the religious sense, i.e., as a matter of conscience before Allah, as opposed to qada'an (judicially enforceable).
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference over analogical reasoning (qiyas) due to need or custom.
شركة في الشراء (shirkah fi al-shira'): Partnership limited to the acquisition of property, not including subsequent sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi istihsan ruling, when may a partner live in the entire joint house in the absence of the other partner?
A) Only if he pays rent for the other's share.
B) Always, and no rent is due even if the house is for investment.
C) Only if he fears the house will fall into disrepair.
D) He may not live in the entire house; only his share.

Key Takeaway
A joint house is considered each partner's full property for dwelling, so a partner may live in the entire house in the other's absence without rent or permission, by virtue of istihsan.

Shirkah — Partnership
intermediate
8 min
Conditions for Valid Mufawada: Equality in Capital, Authority, and Debt
Explains conditions of Mufawada and invalid pairings due to inequality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three required equalities for a valid Mufawada partnership and explain why differences in legal capacity (e.g., Muslim vs. kafir) render it invalid according to the two companions.

Core Idea
Mufawada demands full equality in capital, authority, and liability — any inequality, even in religious capacity, voids the contract.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the strict prerequisites of the Mufawada partnership. Ibn Nujaym clarifies that Mufawada is not just any partnership; it requires partners to be perfectly equal in their ability to transact, their capital, and their debt obligations. This equality extends so far that a partnership between a Muslim and a non-Muslim is invalid because the non-Muslim can engage in trade that the Muslim cannot, breaking the required symmetry. Let’s unpack these conditions and the debates surrounding them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three pillars of Mufawada
The text states Mufawada must include agency and suretyship, and equality in mal (capital), tasarruf (authority to transact), and dayn (debt). Understand each condition and what it entails in practice.
Equality in mal means equal capital value; tasarruf means equal legal capacity to buy/sell; dayn means equal liability for debts.
2
Understand why inequality invalidates
The author gives examples: free vs slave, adult vs minor, Muslim vs kafir. For each, identify which condition(s) fail. For free vs slave, the slave lacks full tasarruf and kafala without master's permission. For adult vs minor, the minor lacks kafala and full tasarruf.
Inequality in any one condition makes the contract batil (void) not just fasid.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on Muslim-kafir Mufawada
Contrast the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) with Abu Yusuf's individual opinion. The two companions argue that the kafir's ability to legally buy wine/pigs gives him an authority the Muslim lacks, breaking equality. Abu Yusuf argues that agency and suretyship are equal regardless of permissible transactions.
The dispute is over whether equality in tasarruf requires identical permissible objects of trade.
4
Apply to other pairs and edge cases
Consider pairs like two slaves, two minors, Muslims with apostates. Use the condition of equality to rule on validity. Note the differences between Mufawada and 'inan in these scenarios.
The ruling may vary depending on whether the inequality is in tasarruf or kafala.

ومسلم وكافر وما يشتريه كل يقع مشتركاً الاطعام أهله وكسوتهم وكل دين لزم أحدهما لأحدهما قوله أن تضمنت وكالة زائد لأنه لا يخص المفاوضة لأن كل عقد شركة يتضمنها ولا تصح إلا بها. والمراد إنما هو بيان خصائصها ولذا ذكر في المحيط أن حكمها صيرورة كل واحد منهما وكيلاً عن صاحبه في التجارة في النصف، وإذا كان لاحدهما دنانير وللآخر دراهم أو سود وللآخر بيض جازت المفاوضة إذا استوت قيمتهما في ظاهر الرواية لأنهما متحدا الجنس من حيث المعنى، وروى الحسن أنه لا يجوز لأن المساواة بينهما لا تعرف إلا بالقيمة وهي مجهولة، وإن تفاضلاً في القيمة لا تجوز المفاوضة في ظاهر الرواية؛ كذا في المحيط قوله : (فلا تصح بين حر وعبد وصبي وبالغ تفريع على اشتراط المساواة في التصرف لأن الحر البالغ يملك التصرف والكفالة والمملوك لا يملك واحداً منهما إلا بإذن المولى والصبي لا يملك الكفالة ولا يملك التصرف إلا بإذن الولي أطلق العبد فشمل المكاتب وأشار إلى أنها لا تصح بين العبدين والمكاتبين والصبيين لأن الصبيين ليسا أهلاً للكفالة ولو بإذن الولي، وأما العبدان وإن كانا أهلاً لها بإذن المولى لكن يتفاضلان فيها لأنهما يتفاوتان في القيمة وقضية المفاوضة صيرورة كل واحد منهما كفيلاً بجميع ما لزم صاحبه ولم يتحقق؛ كذا في المحيط قوله : (ومسلم وكافر) أي لا تصح بينهما لعدم المساواة في الدين وهذا قولهما. وقال أبو يوسف: تجوز للتساوي فإنه لا يبقى بيدها ولا يقر على ملكها بخلاف المسلم وغير المتقرر لا يساوي المتقرر. وقيد بالمسلم والكافر لأنها تجوز بين الذميين وإن كان أحدهما كتابياً والآخر مجوسياً لاستوائهما في التجارة وضمانها لأن الكتابي لو أجر نفسه للذبح يطالب به المجوسي وإن كان لا يقدر على الذبح بنفسه لأنه يقدر عليه بالمعين أو الأجير، وهذا المجوسي لو آجر نفسه للذبح صح الخياط إذا تفاوضا صار كل واحد منهما مطالباً بما على الآخر لأنه يقدر عليه بمعين أو أجير؛ في المحيط. ولو ارتد أحد المتفاوضين بطلت المفاوضة أصلاً وقالا : تصير عناناً؛ كذا كذا كالقصار في
Book of Partnership. It includes agency and suretyship, and they are equal in capital, authority, and debt. So it is not valid between a free man and a slave, nor between a minor and an adult... one like clothes and slaves. End. In al-Tatar Khaniyya: Among the conditions of mufawada is that it be general in all types of trade. Muhammad indicated this in the book, and Shaykh al-Islam mentioned at the end of the chapter on mufawada that it is also permissible for a specific type. End. It is recommended to have witnesses for it, and Muhammad mentioned how to write it: "This is what so-and-so and so-and-so have entered into partnership upon; they have partnered on the basis of piety towards Allah and fulfilling trust; then he states the amount of capital of each and says: 'all of that is in their hands; they buy and sell together and separately, and each acts according to his own opinion, for cash and credit.' And although each owns it by the absolute contract of partnership, yet some scholars say that neither of them owns it, nor sells it, except by explicit mention. So to guard against this, he writes this, then says: 'Whatever profit results is between them according to their capitals, and whatever loss or liability results is likewise.' Summary: What the author mentioned about contract partnership is that it is mufawada, 'inan, acceptance, and aspects. The commentator (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned that they are six, considering that partnership is by capital, by work, and by credit (wujuh). Each is divided into two: mufawada and 'inan. This is the most appropriate view, and it is what the two shaykhs al-Tahawi and al-Karkhi (may Allah have mercy on them) stated, because the first (i.e., the author's classification) implies that the last two are neither mufawada nor 'inan. His statement: (It is mufawada if it includes agency and suretyship, and they are equal in capital, authority, and debt) is an explanation of the first type from the second type. In al-Qamus: 'Mufawada is sharing in everything and equality.' End. Hence it is said in al-Hidaya: 'Because it is a general partnership in all trades; each one entrusts the matter of partnership to his partner absolutely, since it is equality.' One of the poets said: 'People do not prosper if they are disorganized (fawda) without leaders, nor do leaders [prosper] when their ignorant ones become masters.' That is, they are equal. So equality must be achieved at the beginning and end: that is through capital (mal) meaning what partnership is valid in; disparity is not considered in what partnership is not valid for, and likewise in authority (tasarruf), because if one of them possesses an authority that the other does not, equality is lost, and likewise in debt. End. In Fath al-Qadir: His statement 'since it is from equality' (musawah) is lax, because it is a different root; how can derivation be achieved? Rather, it is from tafwid (entrusting) or from fawd (flow) from which 'fada al-ma' (water flowed) when it overflowed and spread. He only meant that its meaning is equality. The apparent meaning of the author's statement is that it is not required to explicitly stipulate mufawada; if they explicitly state it, its rulings are established, substituting the word for the meaning because it has become a sign of complete equality in partnership matters. If they do not mention it, they must mention its complete meaning by one of them saying (while they are both free, adult, Muslim or dhimmis): 'I have entered into partnership with you in all cash and property that you own, on the basis of general delegation from each of us to the other in trades, cash, and credit, and that each is surety for the other regarding whatever is required from him concerning every sale.' And we have stated that it is valid specifically as well, but 'what I own of the price how is it permissible?' So contemplate that. His statement: (and the apparent meaning of the author's statement [to the end]) I say in al-Walwaljiyya the text: 'And no...' page 284. Muslim and disbeliever (kafir), and whatever each buys is jointly owned, including feeding his family and clothing them, and every debt that becomes due to one of them. His statement: 'that it includes agency' is redundant because it is not specific to mufawada, since every partnership contract includes it and is not valid without it. The intent is only to explain its characteristics. Hence in al-Muhit: 'Its ruling is that each becomes an agent for his partner in trade in half (of the capital).' If one has dinars and the other dirhams, or one has black (coins) and the other white (coins), mufawada is permissible if their values are equal, according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), because they are of the same genus in meaning. Hasan narrated that it is not permissible because equality between them is not known except by value, which is unknown. If they differ in value, mufawada is not permissible in the apparent narration; as in al-Muhit. His statement: (so it is not valid between a free man and a slave, nor between a minor and an adult) is a branch of the condition of equality in authority, because the free adult possesses authority and suretyship, while the slave possesses neither except with the master's permission, and the minor does not possess suretyship and does not possess authority except with the guardian's permission. He generalized 'slave' to include the mukatab. And he indicated that it is not valid between two slaves, two mukatabs, or two minors, because two minors are not qualified for suretyship even with the guardian's permission. As for two slaves, even if they are qualified for it with the master's permission, they differ in it because they vary in value, and the requirement of mufawada is that each becomes surety for all that is due from the other, which is not realized. As in al-Muhit. His statement: (and Muslim and disbeliever) i.e., it is not valid between them due to inequality in religion. This is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf said: It is permissible due to equality between them in agency and suretyship, and no consideration is given to additional authority that one possesses, like mufawada between a Shafi'i and a Hanafi, which is permissible; they differ in authority over what is not mentioned (mujmal al-tasmiya?), but it is disliked because the dhimmi does not know the permissible contracts. And for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): There is no equality in authority, for if a dhimmi buys wine or pigs with capital, it is valid, but if a Muslim buys them, it is not valid. He generalized 'disbeliever' to include the apostate (murtad). Hence in al-Muhit: If a Muslim enters into partnership with an apostate as mufawada or 'inan, it is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa if he is killed for apostasy or joins the dar al-harb; if he embraces Islam, it becomes permissible. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), 'inan is permissible but not mufawada. If a Muslim enters into partnership with a female apostate, 'inan is valid but not mufawada. It should be that mufawada is permissible according to Abu Yusuf and disliked, because the actions of the apostate are effective by consensus, so she is equal to the Muslim in trade and its liability, just as with a Muslim and a dhimmi according to him. For the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): Even though she is equal to the Muslim in trade, she is inferior in some benefits derived from trade, for if an apostate buys a Muslim slave or a Quran, it does not remain in her possession nor is it confirmed in her ownership, unlike the Muslim; and that which is not confirmed is not equal to that which is confirmed. He restricted it to Muslim and disbeliever because it (mufawada) is permissible between dhimmis even if one is a Kitabi and the other a Magian, due to their equality in trade and its liability, because if a Kitabi hires himself for slaughtering, the Magian can be held liable for it even if he cannot slaughter himself, because he can do it by assistant or hired worker. And this Magian, if he hires himself for slaughtering, it is valid. For the tailor and the washerman (or cloth-dyer?) if they enter into mufawada, each becomes liable for what is due from the other because he can do it by assistant or hired worker. In al-Muhit. If one of the two partners in mufawada apostatizes, the mufawada is void in the opinion of (Abu Hanifa), and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: It becomes 'inan. As such, as such for the tailor? In (al-Muhit?)?
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the legal conditions for a valid Mufawada partnership (unlimited partnership). The author, Ibn Nujaym, begins by defining Mufawada: it must include reciprocal agency (wakala) and suretyship (kafala), and the partners must be equal in three aspects: capital (mal), authority to transact (tasarruf), and debt (dayn). Equality means each partner has the same capacity to act and be liable. Because of this strict equality, Mufawada is not valid between a free man and a slave, nor between a minor and an adult. The reason: a slave lacks full authority without the master's permission and cannot guarantee suretyship; a minor lacks capacity for suretyship and full authority. Even between two slaves or two minors, equality is absent because their values or capacities differ. Similarly, Mufawada between a Muslim and a non-Muslim (kafir) is invalid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions), because a non-Muslim can legally buy wine or pigs, which a Muslim cannot, so their authority in trade is not equal. However, Abu Yusuf (in a separate opinion) permits it because agency and suretyship are equal, and the difference in permissible transactions is secondary. The passage also covers cases involving apostates (murtad): if a Muslim partners with an apostate, Abu Hanifa holds it invalid entirely if the apostate dies as a renegade or joins dar al-harb; if the apostate returns to Islam, it becomes valid. The two companions allow 'inan (limited partnership) but not Mufawada due to the apostate's potential disability in owning certain items (e.g., a Muslim slave or Quran). The passage emphasizes that Mufawada requires full initial and ongoing equality; any disparity, such as in capital values (e.g., one has dinars, the other dirhams of unequal value) or in legal capacity, invalidates the contract. The author also notes that Mufawada can be valid even if the partners do not explicitly use the word 'mufawada' as long as they state terms that imply complete equality and mutual delegation. Overall, this commentary clarifies the strict prerequisites of Mufawada and the intra-Hanafi differences regarding partnerships with non-Muslims.

Key Terms
المفاوضة (al-mufawada): A type of partnership contract where partners are equal in capital, authority, and liability, and each acts as agent and surety for the other in all trades.
الكفالة (al-kafala): Suretyship or guarantee, where each partner becomes liable for the debts and obligations of the other.
التصرف (al-tasarruf): Legal authority to transact, including buying, selling, and entering contracts. In partnership, it refers to each partner's power to act on behalf of the partnership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is Mufawada between a Muslim and a disbeliever invalid according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad)?
A) Because disbelievers cannot own property.
B) Because the disbeliever can legally buy wine or pigs, which Muslims cannot, creating inequality in authority.
C) Because agency is not possible between a Muslim and a disbeliever.
D) Because Muslim law forbids partnership with non-Muslims in any form.

Key Takeaway
Mufawada demands full and ongoing equality in capital, transactional authority, and debt — any disparity, including religious capacity, nullifies the contract.

Conditions for Valid Mufawada: Equality in Value and Disposition
Commentary on the conditions of equality in mufawada partnership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for valid Hanafi mufawada and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding equality in value.

Core Idea
Mufawada requires equality in both capital value and legal capacity; without both, the contract is invalid.

This commentary from al-Bahr al-Raiq unpacks the conditions for a valid mufawada partnership. While all partnerships share agency, mufawada uniquely requires full equality — in the value of contributed capital and in the capacity to act and guarantee. Understanding these conditions is essential to distinguishing a valid mufawada from other forms of shirkah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two core conditions
Read the passage to extract the two conditions for mufawada: equality in value and equality in legal capacity (disposition and suretyship).
These conditions are derived from the text's discussion of different currencies and the comparison between free/slave, adult/minor.
2
Analyze the value condition and the intra-madhhab dispute
Note that according to Zahir al-Riwayah, different currencies of equal value are allowed because they are one genus in meaning. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad disagrees because equality is known only by value, which is uncertain. Identify the rational argument (qiyas) behind each view.
This step unpacks the 'dalil aqli' reasoning.
3
Analyze the capacity condition
Examine why mufawada is invalid between free and slave, adult and minor, even between two slaves. The key is the requirement of mutual suretyship (kafala), which requires full capacity and equal value.
The text emphasizes that slaves differ in value and minors lack capacity for suretyship.
4
Apply the conditions to examples
Test your understanding by applying the conditions to scenarios: (a) two adults with equal value but different currencies, (b) a free man and a slave with permission, (c) two slaves of different values.
Use the passage's own examples as a template.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence of the dispute
Consider how the opinion of al-Hasan would affect a real transaction: if values are equal but currencies differ, the contract would be invalid under his view, requiring reformation.
The mufta bihi is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله أن تضمنت وكالة زائد لأنه لا يخص المفاوضة لأن كل عقد شركة يتضمنها ولا تصح إلا بها. والمراد إنما هو بيان خصائصها ولذا ذكر في المحيط أن حكمها صيرورة كل واحد منهما وكيلاً عن صاحبه في التجارة في النصف، وإذا كان لاحدهما دنانير وللآخر دراهم أو سود وللآخر بيض جازت المفاوضة إذا استوت قيمتهما في ظاهر الرواية لأنهما متحدا الجنس من حيث المعنى، وروى الحسن أنه لا يجوز لأن المساواة بينهما لا تعرف إلا بالقيمة وهي مجهولة، وإن تفاضلاً في القيمة لا تجوز المفاوضة في ظاهر الرواية؛ كذا في المحيط قوله : (فلا تصح بين حر وعبد وصبي وبالغ تفريع على اشتراط المساواة في التصرف لأن الحر البالغ يملك التصرف والكفالة والمملوك لا يملك واحداً منهما إلا بإذن المولى والصبي لا يملك الكفالة ولا يملك التصرف إلا بإذن الولي أطلق العبد فشمل المكاتب وأشار إلى أنها لا تصح بين العبدين والمكاتبين والصبيين لأن الصبيين ليسا أهلاً للكفالة ولو بإذن الولي، وأما العبدان وإن كانا أهلاً لها بإذن المولى لكن يتفاضلان فيها لأنهما يتفاوتان في القيمة وقضية المفاوضة صيرورة كل واحد منهما كفيلاً بجميع ما لزم صاحبه ولم يتحقق؛ كذا في المحيط
He said: 'That it includes agency as an extra because it is not specific to mufawada since every partnership contract includes it and is not valid without it. The intent is only to explain its characteristics. Therefore it is mentioned in al-Muhit that its ruling is that each one becomes an agent for his companion in trade regarding the half. And if one has dinars and the other has dirhams, or one has black dirhams and the other white ones, mufawada is allowed if their values are equal according to the apparent narration because they are of one genus in meaning. Al-Hasan narrated that it is not allowed because equality between them is known only by value, and that is unknown. And if they differ in value, mufawada is not allowed in the apparent narration. Similarly in al-Muhit. He said: 'So it is not valid between a free man and a slave, nor between a minor and an adult' — this is a branch upon the condition of equality in disposition, because a free adult possesses the right of disposition and suretyship, while a owned person does not possess any of them except with the master's permission; a minor does not possess suretyship nor disposition except with the guardian's permission. He said 'slave' generally, so it includes the mukatab. And he indicated that it is not valid between two slaves or two mukatabs or two minors, because minors are not fit for suretyship even with the guardian's permission. As for two slaves, though they are fit for suretyship with the master's permission, they differ in it because they differ in value, and the requirement of mufawada is that each one becomes a surety for all that obligates his companion, and this is not realized. Similar in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions for the validity of mufawada, a type of partnership where each partner is both agent and surety for the other. The first part discusses the condition of equality in the value of capital contributed. If one partner provides dinars and the other dirhams, or one provides black dirhams and the other white ones, the mufawada is valid as long as the values are equal. This is the position of the Zahir al-Riwayah (the well-known transmission from Abu Hanifa and the two disciples), because all these currencies share the same genus in meaning—they are all money. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated a different opinion that it is not allowed because equality is known only by value, and value is unknown at the time of the contract. However, if the values differ, the mufawada is invalid. The second part discusses the condition of equality in legal capacity to act and provide suretyship. Mufawada is not valid between a free man and a slave, nor between a minor and an adult, because the free adult has full capacity for trade and suretyship, while a slave lacks these without the master's permission, and a minor lacks them even with the guardian's permission. The term 'slave' includes the mukatab (a slave contracting for freedom). The contract is also invalid between two slaves because they differ in value, and the requirement of mutual suretyship cannot be realized.

Key Terms
المفاوضة (al-mufāwaḍah): A type of partnership where each partner is both agent and surety for the other in all transactions, requiring full equality in capital and legal capacity.
ظاهر الرواية (Ẓāhir al-riwāyah): The well-known and authoritative transmission of the Hanafi school from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
الكفالة (al-kafālah): A contract of suretyship where one person guarantees the debt or obligation of another.
الجنس (al-jins): Genus; in this context, the common category of 'money' (thaman) that all currencies share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Zahir al-Riwayah, if one partner contributes gold dinars and the other contributes silver dirhams of equal value, the mufawada is:
A) Valid because they are of one genus in meaning.
B) Invalid because the currencies differ.
C) Valid only if they exchange currencies first.
D) Invalid because equality is unknown.

Key Takeaway
Mufawada requires both equal value of capital and equal legal capacity for disposition and suretyship; otherwise the contract is invalid.

Mufawada Between Muslim and Non-Muslim: Ruling and Proofs
Commentary on the invalidity of mufawada between Muslim and kafir with proofs and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mufawada between a Muslim and a kafir, explain the reason for invalidity according to the two disciples, and compare Abu Yusuf's position.

Core Idea
Mufawada requires full equality in disposition; a dhimmi can trade wine and pigs, so it fails.

The Hanafi school generally rules that mufawada (unlimited partnership) between a Muslim and a non-Muslim (kafir) is invalid. This lesson unpacks the reasoning: the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) base it on inequality in permissible transactions, while Abu Yusuf alone held it valid due to equal agency. We will examine the evidence and apply the rule to apostates and dhimmis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its mainstream Hanafi ruling
Mufawada between a Muslim and a kafir (including dhimmi and apostate) is ruled batil (void) by the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Note that Abu Yusuf disagreed.
The text explicitly states 'لا تصح بينهما' (it is not valid).
2
Understand the primary proof of the two disciples
The key evidence is inequality in disposition (tasarruf). A dhimmi may legally buy wine and pigs, which a Muslim cannot. This breaks the required equality in mufawada.
Compare with 'inan partnership, which does not require such equality.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's counter-argument
Abu Yusuf argued that equality exists in agency and suretyship, and extra dispositions (like buying wine) should be ignored, similar to mufawada between a Hanafi and a Shafi'i.
He considered the contract valid but disliked due to the dhimmi's lack of guidance in permissible contracts.
4
Apply to special cases: apostate and female apostate
The ruling extends to apostates: with a male apostate, according to the two, mufawada is invalid; with a female apostate, similar. Abu Hanifa adds that the contract is suspended until the apostate's status is resolved.
The apostate's ownership of a Muslim slave or Quran is not confirmed, reinforcing inequality.
5
Contrast with dhimmi-dhimmi mufawada
Mufawada between two dhimmis (even different religions like Jewish and Magian) is valid because their permissible trades are equal.
Equality in trade and guarantee exists, as both can hire for slaughter etc.
6
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
The text indicates the fatwa is on the view of the two disciples: mufawada between Muslim and kafir is invalid. If entered, it should be recontracted as 'inan.
From al-Hidaya: wherever mufawada fails, 'inan may stand if its conditions are met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومسلم (وكافر) أي لا تصح بينهما لعدم المساواة في الدين وهذا قولهما. وقال أبو يوسف: تجوز للتساوي بينهما في الوكالة و الكفالة ولا معتبر بزيادة تصرف يملكه أحدهما كالمفاوضة بين الشفعوي والحنفي فإنها جائزة، ويتفاوتان في التصرف في متروك التسمية إلا أنه يكره لأن الذمي لا يهتدي إلى الجائز من العقود ولهما أنه لا تساوي في التصرف فإن الذمي لو اشترى برأس المال خموراً أو خنازير صح ولو اشتراها المسلم لا يصح . أطلق الكافر فشمل المرتد ولذا قال في المحيط : شارك المسلم المرتد مفاوضة أو عناناً لم تجز عند أبي حنيفة إن قتل على ردته أو لحق بدار الحرب وإن أسلم جازت. وعندهما تجوز العنان دون المفاوضة وإن شارك المسلم مرتدة صحت عناناً لا مفاوضة وينبغي أن تجوز المفاوضة عند أبي يوسف وتكره لأن تصرفات المرتدة نافذة بالإجماع فساوت المسلم في التجارات وضمانها كالمسلم مع الذمي عنده لهما أنها وإن ساوت المسلم في التجارات لكنها أدون من المسلم في بعض ما يستفاد بالتجارة فإن المرتدة لو اشترت عبداً مسلماً أو مصحفاً فإنه لا يبقى بيدها ولا يقر على ملكها بخلاف المسلم وغير المتقرر لا يساوي المتقرر. وقيد بالمسلم والكافر لأنها تجوز بين الذميين وإن كان أحدهما كتابياً والآخر مجوسياً لاستوائهما في التجارة وضمانها لأن الكتابي لو أجر نفسه للذبح يطالب به المجوسي وإن كان لا يقدر على الذبح بنفسه لأنه يقدر عليه بالمعين أو الأجير، وهذا المجوسي لو آجر نفسه للذبح صح الخياط إذا تفاوضا صار كل واحد منهما مطالباً بما على الآخر لأنه يقدر عليه بمعين أو أجير؛ في المحيط. ولو ارتد أحد المتفاوضين بطلت المفاوضة أصلاً وقالا : تصير عناناً؛ كذا في التتارخانية معزياً إلى السراجية وذكر قبله أنها موقوفة عنده وأنه يكره للمسلم أن يشارك الذمي اهـ. يعني شركة عنان وفي الهداية وفي كل موضع لم تصح المفاوضة لفقد شرطها ولا يشترط ذلك في العنان كان عناناً لاستجماع شرائط العنان إذ هو قد يكون خاصاً وقد يكون عاماً اهـ . قال في النهاية : بخلاف المفاوضة فإنها عام لا غير اهـ. وفيه ما علمت سابقاً.
He said: '(Muslim and kafir) meaning it is not valid between them due to lack of equality in religion, and this is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Abu Yusuf said: It is allowed because of equality between them in agency and suretyship, and no attention is paid to the extra disposition that one of them possesses, like mufawada between a Shafi'i and a Hanafi, which is allowed, and they differ in disposition in matters not mentioned by name, except that it is disliked because the dhimmi does not guide himself to permissible contracts. Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi ruling on mufawada between a Muslim and a non-Muslim (kafir). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the two disciples), such a partnership is not valid because there is no equality in religion and in the capacity for valid transactions. For example, a dhimmi can legally buy wine or pigs, but a Muslim cannot. However, Abu Yusuf held that it is valid because both are equal in agency and suretyship; he compared it to the permissibility of mufawada between a Shafi'i and a Hanafi. The text further explains that the ruling for an apostate (murtad) follows the same principle: if a Muslim partners with a male apostate, according to Abu Hanifa it is invalid unless the apostate returns to Islam; according to the two, 'inan partnership is allowed but not mufawada. For a female apostate, similar rules apply. The key reason for the two disciples is that the apostate's ownership is not confirmed in certain cases (e.g., buying a Muslim slave or a Quran), so they are unequal. The passage also clarifies that mufawada is allowed between two dhimmis, even if one is a kitabi and the other a magian, because their capabilities in trade and guarantee are equal. Finally, if a mufawada partner apostatizes, Abu Hanifa says the contract becomes void, while the two say it becomes an 'inan partnership. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that mufawada is invalid between Muslim and kafir.

Key Terms
المرتد (al-murtadd): A person who has renounced Islam; in partnership law, their transactions have limited validity.
الذمي (al-dhimmī): A non-Muslim living under Muslim rule with a covenant of protection; they can engage in trade but with restrictions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Mufawada requires full equality in all legal capacities; the potential for a dhimmi to trade in prohibited items invalidates the partnership with a Muslim, and the contract should be restructured as 'inan.

The Istihsan Exception for Necessities in Mufawada
Expansion of the istihsan exception for necessities in mufawada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi exception for personal necessities in mufawada partnership and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
Necessary household purchases in mufawada are exclusive to the buyer, but the partner remains a surety.

In a mufawada partnership, each partner is an agent for the other, so purchases are normally shared. However, for daily necessities like food and clothing, the Hanafi school makes an exception based on istihsan (juristic preference) to avoid impracticality. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, scope, and the continuing liability of the non-buying partner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
First, understand the baseline: in mufawada, each partner is a full agent, so any purchase by one is shared equally.
This is from the contract's requirement of equality (mūjab al-musāwāh).
2
Recognize the Exception and Its Basis
The exception for food and clothing is established by istihsan, not qiyas. The underlying reason is necessity: these are ongoing needs that cannot practically require partner consent or shared fund management.
The text explicitly says 'istihsān' and 'li-l-ḍarūrah'.
3
Grasp the Scope of the Exception
The exception extends beyond food and clothing to other necessities like buying/renting a house for dwelling, hiring transport for Hajj, and (with permission) a female slave for personal service or intercourse.
The key condition is that the purchase is for the personal or family needs of the buying partner.
4
Understand the Continuing Liability (Kafālah)
Even though the purchased item is exclusive, the non-buying partner remains a surety (kafīl) for the price. The seller can claim from either partner, and the surety can recover from the buyer.
This preserves the creditor's rights while maintaining the practical exception.
5
Note the Rule on Separate vs Single Purchases
When partners buy two items with separate capitals in two transactions, each owes half of his own capital to the other as a debt (because of agency), but they do not set off due to different qualities of the capitals. If bought in one transaction, no such debt arises.
This is an added rule from al-Muhit that governs mutual accounting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وما يشتريه كل يقع مشتركاً الاطعام أهله وكسوتهم لأن مقتضى العقد المساواة وكل واحد منهما قائم مقام صاحبه في التصرف فكان شراء أحدهما كشرائهما إلا ما استثناه في الكتاب وهو استحسان لأنه مستثنى عن المفاوضة للضرورة فإن الحاجة الراتبة معلومة الوقوع فلا يمكن إيجابه على صاحبه ولا الصرف من ماله ولا بد من الشراء فيختص به ضرورة، والقياس أن يكون على الشركة لما بينا أراد بالمستثنى ما كان من حوائجه فشمل شراء بيت للسكنى أو الاستئجار للسكنى أو للركوب لحاجته كالحج وغيره، وكذا إلا دام والجارية التي يطؤها بإذن الشريك فليس الكل على الشركة لما ذكرنا وإنما استثنى الطعام وما معه من الشركة دون الضمان لأنه وإن لم يكن على الشركة فالآخر كفيل عنه حتى كان لبائع الطعام والكسوة له ولعياله أن يطالب الآخر ويرجع الآخر بما أدى على المشتري. وإنما قيدنا في الجارية بإذن الشريك لأنه لو اشتراها للوطء أو للخدمة لنفسه بغير إذن شريكه فهي على الشركة كما في المحيط وسنبينه في آخر الباب. وفي المحيط : لو اشتريا بالمالين شيئين صفقتين فلكل واحد منهما على صاحبه نصف رأس ماله ديناً عليه لأن كل واحد صار مشترياً النصف لنفسه والنصف لصاحبه بحكم الوكالة ولا يلتقيان قصاصاً لأن صفة المالين مختلفة بخلاف ما لو اشتريا بالمالين شيئين صفقة واحدة فإنه لا يرجع واحد منهما على صاحبه بشيء لأن كل واحد منهما لم يصر وكيلاً عن صاحبه في ذلك وتمامه فيه
He said: 'And whatever each one purchases is shared except for the food of his family and their clothing, because the requirement of the contract is equality, and each one stands in place of his companion in disposition, so the purchase of one is like the purchase of both, except what he excepted in the book, and this is istihsan because it is excepted from mufawada due to necessity: ongoing needs are known to occur, so it is not possible to oblige it upon his partner nor to spend from his wealth, and purchase is necessary, so it is restricted to him out of necessity. And the qiyas is that it should be on the partnership as we explained. He meant by the exception what is from his needs, so it includes purchasing a house for dwelling, or renting for dwelling, or for riding for his need like Hajj and others. Similarly, the female slave that he has intercourse with by the permission of the partner is not all on the partnership for what we mentioned. He only excepted food and what is with it from partnership, not from guarantee, because even if it is not on the partnership, the other is a surety for him, so the seller of food and clothing to him and his family can demand from the other, and the other can claim what he paid from the purchaser. We only restricted the female slave by the partner's permission because if he buys her for intercourse or for his own service without the partner's permission, she is on the partnership, as in al-Muhit, and we will explain it at the end of the chapter. In al-Muhit: If they buy two things with the two capitals in two transactions, then each one has a claim against the other for half of his capital as a debt because each becomes a purchaser of half for himself and half for his companion by virtue of agency, and they do not set off because the qualities of the two capitals are different. Contrast if they buy two things with the two capitals in one transaction, then no one can claim anything from the other because neither becomes an agent for the other in that. And its completion is in it.'
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage explains an exception in mufawada partnership. Generally, any purchase made by one partner is shared equally between them because each acts as agent for the other. However, by way of istihsan (juristic preference), purchases for the daily necessities of one partner's family, such as food and clothing, are excepted from the shared ownership. This is due to necessity: such needs are ongoing and it would be impractical to require the partner's consent or to draw from the common fund. If the partner needed these items, the other would either have to agree or use his own money, both of which are inconvenient. Therefore, these items belong to the purchasing partner alone, but the other partner remains a surety (kafil) for the price, meaning the seller can claim the price from either partner, and the non-purchasing partner can later recover from the purchaser. The exception also covers purchasing a house for dwelling, renting for housing, or hiring transportation for personal needs like Hajj. For a female slave intended for personal use (intercourse or service) with the partner's permission, the purchase is also not shared; but without permission, it is shared. The passage also mentions a rule about buying two items with two capitals in two different transactions: each partner owes half of his own capital to the other, but they do not set off because the capitals differ in type. If bought in a single transaction, no such debt arises.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsān): A juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling based on necessity or custom.
الكفيل (al-kafīl): A surety who is liable for the debt of another; in this context, the non-purchasing partner is a surety for the price of the food/clothing.
المفاوضة (al-mufāwaḍah): A type of partnership where partners are equal in capital, profit, and liability, and each acts as agent and surety for the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a mufawada partnership, one partner buys food for his family. According to the Hanafi exception, which statement is correct?
A) The food is shared between the partners.
B) The food is exclusively owned by the purchaser, and the other partner has no liability.
C) The food is exclusively owned by the purchaser, but the other partner is a surety for the price.
D) The food is shared only if the other partner gives permission.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan overrides qiyas in mufawada for necessities, but the partner's surety liability continues.

Partnership with a Dhimmi and Exceptions in Mufawada
Commentary on mufawada partnership with two rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on Muslim-dhimmi partnership in the Hanafi school and explain the istihsan exception for family provisions in mufawada.

Core Idea
Mufawada makes all purchases common, but family necessities are exempt by necessity-based istihsan.

This lesson examines two interconnected rulings on mufawada partnership. First, the Hanafi position that partnering with a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) is disliked (makruh tanzih). Second, the rule that while purchases in mufawada become common property, an exception based on istihsan exempts food, clothing, and similar personal necessities. Understanding these rulings reveals how the Hanafi school balances legal principles (qiyas) with practical necessity (istihsan).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Locate the two distinct legal issues: (1) partnership with a dhimmi, (2) exception for family provisions in mufawada. Note that the first is about who may partner, the second about what remains private.
The passage begins with a citation from al-Tartarkhani about dhimmi partnership.
2
Analyze the dhimmi partnership ruling
Determine why it is disliked (makruh). The text does not give a detailed proof, but it is based on potential conflicts in dealings. The ruling is from later authorities (fatawa mutakhkhirin) and is not from the six core books.
Compare with other schools: some allow it unconditionally.
3
Understand the general rule in mufawada
In valid mufawada, each partner is agent and guarantor for the other. Thus any purchase by one belongs to both equally. This is the default ruling based on equality and mutual agency.
Contrast with 'inan partnership where such universality is absent.
4
Examine the istihsan exception
The text states that purchases of food and clothing for one's family are excepted from common ownership. The reason is necessity: such needs are recurring and unavoidable; imposing them on the partner would be impractical. Hence istihsan overrides qiyas.
The exception extends to renting a house, riding animals for Hajj, and a slave-girl bought with permission for intercourse.
5
Note the guarantee (daman) aspect
Though the purchased item belongs solely to the partner, the other partner remains a guarantor for the price. The seller can demand payment from either, but the non-purchasing partner can then recover from the purchaser.
This preserves the mutual guarantee inherent in mufawada.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التتارخانية معزياً إلى السراجية وذكر قبله أنها موقوفة عنده وأنه يكره للمسلم أن يشارك الذمي اهـ. يعني شركة عنان وفي الهداية وفي كل موضع لم تصح المفاوضة لفقد شرطها ولا يشترط ذلك في العنان كان عناناً لاستجماع شرائط العنان إذ هو قد يكون خاصاً وقد يكون عاماً اهـ . قال في النهاية : بخلاف المفاوضة فإنها عام لا غير اهـ. وفيه ما علمت سابقاً. قوله : (وما يشتريه كل يقع مشتركاً الاطعام أهله وكسوتهم لأن مقتضى العقد المساواة وكل واحد منهما قائم مقام صاحبه في التصرف فكان شراء أحدهما كشرائهما إلا ما استثناه في الكتاب وهو استحسان لأنه مستثنى عن المفاوضة للضرورة فإن الحاجة الراتبة معلومة الوقوع فلا يمكن إيجابه على صاحبه ولا الصرف من ماله ولا بد من الشراء فيختص به ضرورة، والقياس أن يكون على الشركة لما بينا أراد بالمستثنى ما كان من حوائجه فشمل شراء بيت للسكنى أو الاستئجار للسكنى أو للركوب لحاجته كالحج وغيره، وكذا إلا دام والجارية التي يطؤها بإذن الشريك فليس الكل على الشركة لما ذكرنا وإنما استثنى الطعام وما معه من الشركة دون الضمان لأنه وإن لم يكن على الشركة فالآخر كفيل عنه حتى كان لبائع الطعام والكسوة له ولعياله أن يطالب الآخر ويرجع الآخر بما أدى على المشتري. وإنما قيدنا في الجارية بإذن الشريك لأنه لو اشتراها للوطء أو للخدمة لنفسه بغير إذن شريكه فهي على الشركة كما في المحيط وسنبينه في آخر الباب.
The Tartarkhaniyah, citing the Sirajiyah, mentioned before that it is suspended with him and that it is disliked for a Muslim to partner with a dhimmi. Meaning 'inan partnership. And in al-Hidayah: In every place where mufawada is not valid due to lack of a condition, that condition is not required in 'inan, so it becomes 'inan because it fulfills the conditions of 'inan, since it may be specific or general. He said in al-Nihayah: Unlike mufawada, which is general only. In it, you already know. He said: (And whatever each buys falls commonly shared, except for feeding his family and clothing them. Because the import of the contract is equality, and each one stands in the place of his partner in disposal, so the purchase of one is like the purchase of both, except what the book excepted, which is istihsan because it is excepted from mufawada due to necessity. For the recurring need is known to occur, so it is not possible to impose it on his partner nor to spend from his wealth, and purchase is inevitable, so it is specific to him by necessity. And the analogy is that it should be on the partnership, as we stated. By the excepted, he intended what is from his needs, so it includes purchasing a house for dwelling, or renting for dwelling, or for riding for his need like Hajj and others. Likewise, except for the slave-girl he has intercourse with by the partner's permission, so not all is on the partnership for what we mentioned. He only excepted food and what is with it from the partnership, not from guarantee, because even though it is not on the partnership, the other is a guarantor for him, so the seller of food and clothing for him and his family can demand from the other, and the other can recover what he paid from the purchaser. We only restricted the slave-girl to the partner's permission because if he bought her for intercourse or for service for himself without his partner's permission, she is on the partnership, as in al-Muhit, and we will explain it at the end of the chapter.)
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding mufawada (universal partnership). It begins by addressing a subsidiary issue: it is disliked (makruh) for a Muslim to enter into an 'inan partnership with a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject). The author clarifies that the referenced discussion assumes 'inan partnership, not mufawada, as mufawada requires specific conditions. The core ruling then addresses the general rule that in valid mufawada, any purchase made by one partner becomes common property between them, since each acts as agent and guarantor for the other. However, there is an exception made by istihsan (juridical preference) for purchases of food and clothing for the partner's own family. The reason is necessity: such needs are recurring and inevitable, and it would be impractical to require the partner's consent or to debit the partnership. By analogy (qiyas), these purchases would be on the partnership, but the exception stands due to necessity. The exception extends to purchasing or renting a house for dwelling, riding animals for personal travel like Hajj, and a slave-girl bought for intercourse with the partner's permission. In all these cases, the item belongs to the purchasing partner alone, not the partnership. However, the guarantee (daman) for the price still falls on both partners—the seller can sue either, and the non-purchasing partner can recover from the purchaser. The restriction regarding the slave-girl is important: if bought without permission, she becomes partnership property. This exception is limited to personal necessities; other purchases remain common.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufawada): A universal partnership where each partner is agent and guarantor for the other in all dealings, requiring capital equality and full authority.
عنان ('inan): A limited partnership where each partner contributes capital but acts independently within defined scope, not as universal agent.
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference departing from qiyas due to necessity or custom, used here to exempt family provisions from common ownership.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee or liability; in mufawada each partner is a guarantor for the other's purchases, even if the item is excepted from common ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It belongs solely to the purchasing partner, but the other partner is still liable for the price.
C) It belongs solely to the purchasing partner, and the other partner has no liability.
D) It is considered a loan from the partnership to the partner.

Key Takeaway
Mufawada creates common ownership of purchases, but istihsan exempts personal necessities for the family, while preserving mutual guarantee for the price.
