Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Guarantee (Kafalah) in Mufawada Partnership
Commentary on debts binding both partners in mufawada with intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that debts from trade, usurpation, and guarantee bind both mufawada partners, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a guarantee given by one partner binds the other.

Core Idea
In mufawada, trade and usurpation debts bind both partners; guarantees are disputed: Abu Hanifa says yes, the Sahibayn says only with the debtor's order.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq elaborates on the classic Hanafi view that in a mufawada partnership, each partner is both agent and guarantor for the other, so debts from trade, usurpation, and certain guarantees bind both. However, a key intra-madhhab difference emerges over kafalah (suretyship). Understanding this dispute and its underlying principles will sharpen your ability to analyze partnership liability in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the General Rule
Identify the matn's claim: every debt that becomes binding on one partner through trade, usurpation, or guarantee also binds the other partner. This is because each is a guarantor (kafil) for the other.
2
Classify the Types of Debts
Distinguish what falls under 'trade' (price in valid/void sales, rent), 'usurpation' (including denial of trust, consumption, loan for use), and 'guarantee' (kafalah with order).
Usurpation is expanded to include any wrongful taking that leads to compensation akin to trade.
3
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Kafalah
Examine the disagreement: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that a guarantee (with order) binds the partner because ultimately it is compensatory. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it does not because it is a gratuitous act (tabarru'), as evidenced by its invalidity from minors and slaves.
The disagreement hinges on whether the ultimate compensatory nature overrides the initial gift character.
4
Evaluate the Proofs (Dalil)
For Abu Hanifa: the guarantee leads to the guarantor's right to recover from the principal debtor, making it a compensated act in conclusion. For the Sahibayn: it is invalid from those lacking capacity, proving it is pure donation.
Consider the textual evidence from the passage: minors, insane, slaves, and the rule of validity from one-third for a sick person.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi (Operative Ruling)
The passage implies that when the guarantee is with the debtor's order, Abu Hanifa's view is followed; without order, the Sahibayn's view applies. This is the cautious position.
Other issues like acknowledgment (iqrar) and loans are also discussed but as secondary points.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

لحاجة التجارة والمراد بالغصب ما يشبه ضمان التجارة فيدخل ضمان الاستهلاك والوديعة المجحودة أو المستهلكة، وكذا العارية لأن تقرر الضمان في هذه المواضع يفيد له تملك الأصل فيصير في معنى التجارة، وأما لزوم صاحبه بكفالته فهو قول الإمام . وقالا : لا يلزمه لأنه تبرع ولهذا لا يصح من الصبي والمجنون والعبد المأذون والمكاتب، ولو صدر من المريض يصح من الثلث وصار كالإقراض والكفالة بالنفس. ولأبي حنيفة أنه تبرع ابتداء ومعاوضة انتهاء لأنه يستوجب الضمان بما يؤدي عن المكفول عنه إذا كانت الكفالة بأمره فبالنظر إلى البقاء تتضمنه المفاوضة وبالنظر إلى الابتداء لم يصح ممن ذكره وصح من الثلث من المريض بخلاف الكفالة بالنفس لأنه تبرع ابتداء وانتهاء. أما الإقراض فعن أبي حنيفة أنه يلزم صاحبه ولو سلم فهو إعارة فيكون لمثلها حكم عينها لا حكم البدل حتى لا يصح فيه الأجل فلا تتحقق مفاوضة؛ كذا في الهداية وفي المحيط : لو استقرض أحدهما لزم الآخر في ظاهر الرواية وليس لأحدهما الإقراض في ظاهر الرواية، ولو كانت الكفالة بغير أمره لم يلزم صاحبه في الصحيح لانعدام معنى المعاوضة، ومطلق الجواب في الكتاب محمول على المقيد وهو الكفالة بأمر المكفول عنه . وقيد بالثلاث احترازاً عن أرش الجنايات على بني آدم والمهر في النكاح وبدل الخلع والصلح عن دم العمد وعن النفقة لأن هذه الأشياء فيها الاشتراك بخلاف الثلاثة فإنه يصح فيها الاشتراك وإن لم تكن على الشركة كطعام أهله. وفي القاموس : التاجر الذي يبيع ويشتري والجمع تجار وتجار وتجر وتجر كرجال وعمال وصحب وكتب وقد تجر تجراً وتجارة .اهـ. ولو قال المصنف وكل شيء دون أن يقول لكان أولى ليشمل ما إذا آجر أحد المتفاوضين عبداً فإن للمستأجر مطالبة الآخر بتسليم العبد كما أن للآخر أخذ الأجرة بخلاف ما إذا آجر عبداً من ميراث أو شيئاً له خاصة ليس لشريكه أخذ الأجرة ولا للمستأجر مطالبته بتسليم المستأجر والفرق أن كل واحد منهما وكيل عن صاحبه في قبض الديون الواجبة في التجارة وكفيل بما وجب عليه بسبب التجارة وإجارة العبد من تجارتهما من باب التجارة فصار كل واحد مطالباً ومطالباً، فأما إجارة عبد له خاصة خرجت عن المفاوضة للضرورة بخلاف ما لو أجر أحدهما نفسه لأن منافعه داخلة تحت المفاوضة، ولا تبطل المفاوضة إذا آجر عبد الميراث وإن كانت الأجرة نقداً إلا إذا قبضها لأن الدين لا تصح الشركة فيه ؛ كذا في المحيط .
کل دین
وأطلق في لزوم الثلاثة فشمل ما إذا لزم أحدهما بإقراره فإنه يكون عليهما لأنه أخبر عن أمر يملك استئنافه ؛ كذا في المحيط إلا إذا أقر لمن لا تقبل شهادته له فإنه يلزمه خاصة كأصوله وفروعه وامرأته. وعندهما يلزم شريكه أيضاً إلا لعبده ومكاتبه. ولو أقر المعتدته المبانة لم يصح عند أبي حنيفة، وروى الحسن أنه يصح بناء على أنه لا تقبل شهادته لمعتدته في ظاهر الرواية، وفي رواية الحسن تقبل ولو أعتق أم ولده ثم أقر لها بدين يلزمهما وإن كانت في عدته بخلاف المبانة المعتدة والفرق أن شهادته لأم ولده المعتقة جائزة بخلاف المعتدة عن نكاح وتمامه في المحيط . وإذا باع أحد المتفاوضين من صاحبه ثوباً من شريكه ليقطعه قميصاً لنفسه جاز بخلاف ما إذا باع أحدهما من صاحبه شيئاً من الشركة لأجل التجارة حيث لا يجوز، وكذلك لو باع جارية ليطأها أو طعاماً ليجعله رزقاً لأهله جاز البيع؛ كذا في الظهيرية. وهذا يستثنى من قوله ما لزم أحدهما بالتجارة لزم الآخر فإن المشتري من شريكه في صورة جواز البيع لزمه الثمن ولم يلزم شريكه فيقال إلا إذا كان الدائن الشريك كما لا تصح الشركة إلا بلفظ المفاوضة ليكون اللفظ دليلاً على معنى العموم .اهـ. قوله : (احترازاً عن أرشي الجنايات على بني آدم قال في النهر أما الجناية على الدابة أو الثوب فتلزمه في قول الإمام ومحمد لما
He said: (And every debt that becomes binding on one of them through trade, usurpation, and guarantee becomes binding on the other because he is a guarantor. So included under trade are the price of a purchase in a valid sale and its value in an invalid one, whether it is partnership property or for himself, and the rent of what he leased, whether he leased it for himself or for the need of trade. By usurpation is meant what resembles a trade guarantee, so it includes guarantee for consumption, a denied or consumed trust, likewise a loan for use because establishment of guarantee in these places gives him ownership of the original, so it becomes akin to trade. As for the other being bound by his guarantee, that is the statement of Imam (Abu Hanifa). The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It does not bind him because it is a gratuitous act, and for this reason it is not valid from a child, an insane person, a permitted slave, and a mukatab. If it comes from a sick person, it is valid from one-third and becomes like a loan and guarantee of the person. According to Abu Hanifa, it is a gratuitous act initially and a compensatory act ultimately because he becomes entitled to recovery from the person guaranteed for what he pays if the guarantee was by his order. So with regard to the outcome, mufawada includes it; with regard to initiation, it is not valid from those mentioned, but it is valid from one-third from the sick person, unlike guarantee of the person because it is a gratuitous act both initially and ultimately. As for a loan, it is reported from Abu Hanifa that it binds his partner. Even if it is a loan, it is a loan for use, so it has the ruling of its original, not the ruling of a substitute, so that a term is not valid in it, and thus mufawada is not realized. Thus in al-Hidayah. And in al-Muhit: If one of them borrows money, it binds the other in the apparent narration, and neither of them may give a loan in the apparent narration. If the guarantee was without his order, it does not bind his partner in the correct view due to the absence of the meaning of compensation. The absolute response in the book is restricted to the case of guarantee by order of the guaranteed person. And he restricted it to the three to exclude compensation for injuries to humans, dowry in marriage, consideration for khul', and settlement for intentional bloodshed, and maintenance, because in these things there is commonality contrary to the three, where sharing is valid even if not on the partnership, like food for his family. In al-Qamus: The merchant is one who buys and sells; the plural is tujjar, tujjar, tujur, tujur like rijal, 'ummal, suhub, kutub; and he has engaged in trade, trading and trade. He said: And if the author had said 'and everything' instead of saying, it would have been better to include when one of the two mutafawidan leases a slave, for the lessee may demand the other to hand over the slave, just as the other may take the rent, unlike if he leases a slave from inheritance or something specific to him; his partner may not take the rent, nor may the lessee demand him to hand over the leased item. The difference is that each one is an agent for his partner in receiving debts owed in trade and a guarantor for what is owed due to trade, and leasing a slave from their trade is from the category of trade, so each becomes a demander and demanded. As for leasing a slave specific to him, it exits from mufawada due to necessity, unlike if one of them hires himself, because his benefits are included in mufawada. And mufawada does not become invalid if he leases a slave of inheritance even if the rent is cash, unless he receives it, because partnership is not valid in a debt. Thus in al-Muhit. Every debt. And he spoke absolutely about the binding of the three, so it includes when a debt becomes binding on one of them by his acknowledgment, for it is upon both because he is informing about a matter he is able to initiate. Thus in al-Muhit, except if he acknowledges to someone whose testimony is not acceptable for him, then it binds him specifically, like his ascendants, descendants, and wife. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it also binds his partner except for his slave and mukatab. If he acknowledges to his divorced wife during her waiting period, it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. It is reported from Hasan that it is valid based on the fact that his testimony for his divorced wife is not accepted in the apparent narration, but in Hasan's narration it is accepted. And if he frees his umm walad then acknowledges a debt to her, it binds both, even if she is in her waiting period, unlike the divorced woman in waiting. The difference is that his testimony for his freed umm walad is permissible, unlike the divorced woman from marriage. And its completion is in al-Muhit. If one of the two mutafawidan sells to his partner a garment from their partnership for him to cut into a shirt for himself, it is permissible, unlike if one of them sells to his partner something from the partnership for the purpose of trade, where it is not permissible. Likewise, if he sells a slave-girl for him to have intercourse with, or food for him to make it provision for his family, the sale is permissible. Thus in al-Zahiriyyah. This is an exception from his statement: whatever becomes binding on one of them by trade binds the other. For the buyer from his partner in the case of a permissible sale, the price becomes binding on him and does not bind his partner. So it is said: except when the creditor is the partner, just as partnership is only valid with the wording of mufawada so that the wording is evidence of the meaning of generality. He said: (As a caution against compensation for injuries to humans, he said in al-Nahr: As for injury to an animal or garment, it binds him according to the statement of Imam (Abu Hanifa) and Muhammad because...
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn's ruling that any debt arising from trade, usurpation (ghasb), or guarantee (kafalah) that becomes binding on one mufawada partner also binds the other, because each is both agent and guarantor. The author first explains what falls under 'trade' (tijarah): the price of a purchase in a valid sale, the value in a void sale (whether partnership property or personal), and rental payments for anything leased (for self or trade). 'Usurpation' here includes guarantees for consumption, denied trusts, consumed trusts, and loans for use (ariyah), because establishing guarantee in these cases effectively gives the guarantor ownership, bringing it under trade. The main intra-Hanafi dispute is on kafalah (surety/guarantee). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that if one partner guarantees a debt with the order of the debtor, it binds the other partner because ultimately it involves compensation, even though initially it is a gift. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue it does not bind because it is pure donation (tabarru'), and hence invalid from minors, slaves, etc. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Sahibayn unless the guarantee was with the debtor's order, in which case Abu Hanifa's view applies. The text also addresses lending (iqrad): according to Abu Hanifa, if one partner lends money, it binds the other; the Sahibayn consider it invalid unless it is essentially a loan for use. The passage then discusses acknowledgment of debt (iqrar): generally, if one partner acknowledges a debt, it binds both, except when the creditor is someone whose testimony is not accepted (e.g., close relatives). The author concludes with a ruling on sale between partners in mufawada: if one sells to the other for personal use (like a garment for a shirt, slave-girl for intercourse, food for family), the sale is valid and the price binds the buyer alone, not the partnership. But if sold for trade, it is invalid because the partner cannot be both buyer and seller in the same transaction. This is an exception to the general rule that trade debts bind both. The final lines caution that the three exceptions (food, clothing, etc.) exclude liabilities like injury compensation, dower, khul' consideration, blood money, and maintenance, as these are inherently personal.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A guarantee or suretyship where a person assumes responsibility for another's debt or obligation.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; wrongful taking of property, but here expanded to include any wrongful acquisition that entails a guarantee similar to trade.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a debt or obligation, which in mufawada generally binds both partners unless the creditor is a close relative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), why does a guarantee given by one mufawada partner not bind the other?
A) Because it is a compensatory act initially.
B) Because it is a gratuitous act (tabarru') both initially and ultimately.
C) Because mufawada does not include guarantees.
D) Because it is invalid without the partner's consent.

Key Takeaway
In mufawada, debts from trade and usurpation bind both partners; guarantees bind both only if with the debtor's order, per the preferred view.

Shirkah — Partnership
intermediate
6 min
The Three Binding Obligations in Mufawada and Their Exceptions
Expansion and application of a ruling on Mufawada obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three obligations that bind both partners in Mufawada, explain the rationale based on mutual agency and suretyship, and apply the rule to hiring out partnership property versus personal property.

Core Idea
In Mufawada, only trade-related obligations—sale, loan, suretyship—bind both partners; personal debts like mahr and blood money do not.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on a key rule in Hanafi partnership law. The base text of Kanz al-Daqaiq states that only three types of obligations bind both partners in a Mufawada partnership. The commentator clarifies the reasoning and extends it to the case of hiring (ijarah), showing how the principle applies in practice. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly determining liability in equal partnerships.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three binding obligations and the exceptions
Read the passage and note the three obligations listed: sale (al-bay‘), loan (al-qard), and suretyship (al-kafalah). Also note the exceptions: blood money (arsh), dower (mahr), consideration for khul‘ (badal al-khul‘), compromise for deliberate homicide (sulh ‘an dam al-‘amd), and maintenance (nafaqah).
These exceptions involve personal liability, not trade.
2
Understand the rationale: Mufawada as mutual agency and suretyship
The commentator explains that each partner in Mufawada is an agent (wakil) and a surety (kafil) for the other in trade matters. Therefore, trade debts become binding on both. The exceptions do not arise from trade and thus do not bind the partner.
The illah is the trade context; personal obligations have no connection to the partnership.
3
Apply the rule to ijarah: distinguish partnership assets from personal assets
If one partner hires out a slave that is part of the partnership's trade assets, both partners are bound: the lessee can demand delivery from either, and either can collect the hire. If the slave is personal property (e.g., inherited), only the individual partner is liable.
The distinction hinges on whether the asset belongs to the partnership (trade) or to the individual.
4
Note the exception for hiring out oneself and inherited slaves
Hiring out one's own labour is included under the partnership because personal services fall within the scope of trade. Hiring out an inherited slave does not invalidate the partnership unless the hire is actually received (since a debt cannot be jointly owned).
This shows the subtlety of the rule in edge cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بالثلاث احترازاً عن أرش الجنايات على بني آدم والمهر في النكاح وبدل الخلع والصلح عن دم العمد وعن النفقة لأن هذه الأشياء فيها الاشتراك بخلاف الثلاثة فإنه يصح فيها الاشتراك وإن لم تكن على الشركة كطعام أهله. وفي القاموس : التاجر الذي يبيع ويشتري والجمع تجار وتجار وتجر وتجر كرجال وعمال وصحب وكتب وقد تجر تجراً وتجارة .اهـ. ولو قال المصنف وكل شيء دون أن يقول لكان أولى ليشمل ما إذا آجر أحد المتفاوضين عبداً فإن للمستأجر مطالبة الآخر بتسليم العبد كما أن للآخر أخذ الأجرة بخلاف ما إذا آجر عبداً من ميراث أو شيئاً له خاصة ليس لشريكه أخذ الأجرة ولا للمستأجر مطالبته بتسليم المستأجر والفرق أن كل واحد منهما وكيل عن صاحبه في قبض الديون الواجبة في التجارة وكفيل بما وجب عليه بسبب التجارة وإجارة العبد من تجارتهما من باب التجارة فصار كل واحد مطالباً ومطالباً، فأما إجارة عبد له خاصة خرجت عن المفاوضة للضرورة بخلاف ما لو أجر أحدهما نفسه لأن منافعه داخلة تحت المفاوضة، ولا تبطل المفاوضة إذا آجر عبد الميراث وإن كانت الأجرة نقداً إلا إذا قبضها لأن الدين لا تصح الشركة فيه؛ كذا في المحيط.
It is restricted to three [things] as a precaution against the blood-money of injuries on humans, the dower in marriage, the consideration for khul', the compromise for deliberate bloodshed, and maintenance, because these things involve participation [mixing rights], unlike the three [sale, loan, suretyship] where participation is valid even if not [arising] from the partnership, like the food of his family. In al-Qamus: The merchant who buys and sells; the plural is tujjar, tujjar, tujar, tujar, like rijal, 'ummal, suhb, and kutub; and he has traded, trading and commerce. End. If the author had said 'and everything' without saying 'something', it would have been better to include the case where one of the two partners hires out a slave, for the lessee may demand delivery of the slave from the other [partner], just as the other may take the hire. Unlike if he hires out a slave from inheritance or something solely his own; his partner cannot take the hire, nor can the lessee demand delivery from him. The difference is that each of them is an agent for his partner in collecting debts owed in trade, and a surety for what becomes obligatory on him due to trade. Hiring out the slave from their trade is from the category of trade, so each becomes a claimant and a defendant. As for hiring out a slave that belongs exclusively to him, it is outside the partnership out of necessity. Unlike if one of them hires out himself, because his benefits are included under the partnership. The partnership is not invalidated if he hires out a slave of inheritance, even if the hire is cash, unless he accepts it, because a debt is not validly subject to partnership. Thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq about the obligations that bind both partners in a Mufawada (equal) partnership. The base text states that only three types of obligations bind both partners: sale, loan, and suretyship (al-bay', al-qard, al-kafalah). The commentator explains that these three are exceptions to the general rule that each partner is only liable for his own actions. The reason (talil) is that Mufawada is built on mutual agency (wakalah) and suretyship (kafalah) in matters of trade. Therefore, any debt arising from trade—such as a price from sale, a loan, or a surety for a trade debt—binds both. In contrast, obligations like blood money (arsh), dower (mahr), consideration for khul', and compensation for deliberate homicide do not bind the partner because they involve personal participation (ishtirak) and are not part of trade. The commentator then applies this principle to hiring (ijarah): if one partner hires out a slave that is part of the partnership's trade assets, both partners are bound—the lessee can demand delivery from either partner, and either partner can collect the hire. If the slave is personal property (e.g., inherited or exclusively owned), only the individual partner is liable; the other partner has no claim to the hire and is not required to deliver. This distinction arises because trade assets are subject to the mutual agency inherent in Mufawada, while personal assets are not. The passage also clarifies that hiring out one's own labor is included in the partnership because personal services fall under the scope of trade, whereas hiring out a personal slave is an exception due to necessity. Finally, it notes that if one partner hires out an inherited slave, the partnership remains valid unless and until the hire is actually received, because a debt (the right to collect hire) cannot be jointly owned.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): A type of equal partnership where each partner is agent and surety for the other in matters of trade, with full equality in capital and authority.
أرش (arsh): Compensation (blood money) for injuries to humans, assessed on the degree of harm.
كفالة (kafālah): Suretyship; a contract guaranteeing the debt or presence of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following obligations binds both partners in a Mufawada partnership according to the Hanafi school?
A) The dower (mahr) of a wife married by one partner
B) A loan taken by one partner for trade purposes
C) Blood money (arsh) for an injury caused by one partner
D) Maintenance (nafaqah) for the partner's family

Key Takeaway
In Mufawada, only obligations arising from trade (sale, loan, suretyship) bind both partners because the partnership is built on mutual agency and suretyship for trade.

General Rule and Exception for Close Relatives
Ruling on admission in Mufawada with the first exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule that a Mufawada partner's admission binds both, and identify the exception for close relatives, explaining the Hanafi reasoning.

Core Idea
A Mufawada partner's admission binds both, unless the admission is to someone whose testimony is rejected.

The partnership of Mufawada is built on mutual agency. When one partner admits a debt from trade, it binds both because he could have initiated the obligation himself. However, this binding effect does not apply to close relatives. In this lesson, we analyze the rule and its first exception.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
The author states that if one partner admits a debt, it binds both because he informed about a matter he is capable of initiating.
The rule is based on the talīl (reasoning) that the admission is like a voluntary creation of the debt.
2
Identify the exception
The exception is when the admission is made to someone whose testimony is not accepted for the admitter, such as ascendants, descendants, or his wife; then it binds only the admitter.
This is because the admission is to a person whose testimony cannot be used to confirm the claim, so the partner is not bound.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sāḥibayn), it also binds the partner even in the exception, except for the admitter's slave or mukātab.
This shows a difference within the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the ruling
Given a scenario, decide whether the admission binds both or only the admitter.
Use the criterion: testimony admissibility for the admitter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He [the author] made the binding of the three things absolute, so it includes when an obligation becomes due on one of them through his admission, for it is upon both, because he informed about a matter that he is capable of initiating. Thus in al-Muhit, except if he admits to someone whose testimony is not accepted for him, then it binds only him, like his parents, children, and wife. According to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], it also binds his partner, except for his slave and his mukatab. If he admits to his divorced wife who is irrevocably divorced, it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. Al-Hasan narrated that it is valid, based on the fact that his testimony for his divorced woman is not accepted in the apparent narration, and in al-Hasan's narration it is accepted. If he emancipates his umm walad and then admits a debt to her, it binds both, even if she is in her waiting period, unlike the irrevocably divorced woman in her waiting period. The difference is that his testimony for his emancipated umm walad is permissible, unlike for a woman divorced from marriage. Its completion is in al-Muhit. If one of the two partners sells a garment to his partner from [the stock of] his partner to cut it into a shirt for himself, it is permissible, unlike if one of them sells to his partner something from the partnership for the purpose of trade, for it is not permissible. Similarly, if he sells a slave-girl to have intercourse with her, or food to make it provisions for his family, the sale is permissible. Thus in al-Zahiriyya. This is an exception to his statement: 'What becomes binding on one through trade binds the other,' for the buyer from his partner in the permissible sale scenario is liable for the price, but his partner is not liable. So it is said: except when the creditor is the partner, just as partnership is not valid except with the word mufawada so that the word is evidence of the meaning of generality. End. His statement: 'As a precaution against the blood money for injuries on humans.' He said in al-Nahr: As for injury to an animal or garment, it binds him according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] and Muhammad, as is obvious. And the author indicated by the binding of the three types that if a claim is made against one of them, and the claimant wants to administer an oath to the other, he may do so. Al-Walwaliji said in his Fatawa: If someone claims against one of the two partners and he denies, so he is sworn, and then the claimant wants to swear the other, the judge swears him on his knowledge, because the claim against one is a claim against both. If he claims something against both, he may swear each of them absolutely, because each is sworn concerning his own act. Whichever of them refuses the oath, the matter is finalized against both, because the admission of one is like the admission of both. If he claims against one while he is absent, he may swear the present one on his knowledge, because it is the act of another. If he swears and then the absent one arrives, he may swear him absolutely, because he swears him concerning his own act. If a man claims against one of the two partners for a wound by mistake which has blood money, and swears him absolutely, and he swears, then he wants to swear his partner, he may not do so. Similarly for dower, khul', and compromise for deliberate bloodshed, because these things are not included under the partnership, so the act of one is not like the act of both. End. And his statement 'by trade' includes the dower of a purchased slave-girl who is cohabited with if she is recovered. He said in al-Zahiriyya: If one of the two partners cohabits with a purchased slave-girl and then the slave-girl is recovered, the rightful owner may take whichever he wishes for the 'uqr [indemnity], and this is not like the dower in marriage, because the 'uqr here became obligatory due to trade, unlike the dower. End. If the author had said after this general rule: 'And everything established for one of them through trade or the like, the other may take possession of it and demand it,' it would be more beneficial, due to what is in al-Zahiriyya: If one of the two partners sells a slave or gives a loan to a man, or a man gives him a surety for a debt, or he usurps property, his partner may demand [it]. But if something belongs exclusively to one of them, and he sells it, his partner may not demand the price, nor may the buyer demand delivery from the partner.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage expands on the earlier ruling about the three obligations that bind both partners. It clarifies that an admission (iqrar) by one partner concerning a trade debt binds both partners, because he has informed about a matter he is capable of initiating (i.e., he could have created the obligation himself). However, an exception is made if the admission is made to someone whose testimony would not be accepted for him, such as his ascendants, descendants, or wife—in that case, it binds only the admitting partner. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), it also binds the partner except in the case of the admitting partner's slave or mukatab. A further divergence is noted regarding admission to a divorced woman during her waiting period: Abu Hanifa does not validate it, while al-Hasan ibn Ziyad's narration holds it valid based on differing views on the admissibility of testimony for such a woman. The passage then discusses a sale between partners: if one partner sells a garment from the partnership stock to the other partner for the latter's personal use (e.g., to make a shirt), the sale is valid because it is not a trade transaction. But if the sale is for resale as part of trade, it is invalid because it would involve buying and selling within the same partnership without real change of ownership. Similarly, selling a slave-girl for intercourse or food for personal consumption is valid. This is an exception to the general rule that what binds one through trade binds the other—here, the buyer is personally liable for the price, but the seller's partner is not. The passage then discusses procedural aspects: if a claim is made against one partner, the claimant may swear the other partner on his knowledge because the claim is effectively against both. But if the claim is for something not arising from trade (like blood money, dower, etc.), the other partner cannot be sworn because those matters are not part of the partnership. Finally, the passage notes that if a purchased slave-girl is cohabited with and then found to belong to another, the 'uqr (indemnity) binds both partners because it arose from trade (the purchase), unlike dower in marriage. The commentator suggests that the author could have articulated a more comprehensive rule: everything that becomes due to one partner through trade or similar transactions, the other may also claim and take possession. Examples include sale, loan, surety, and usurpation—all bind both. But if the property belonged exclusively to one partner, the other has no right to the price or delivery.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Admission or confession of a debt or fact; in Mufawada, it binds both partners if it concerns trade.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom from his master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Mufawada partnership, if one partner admits a debt to his wife, what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The debt binds both partners because admission is binding on both
B) The debt binds only the admitting partner because the wife's testimony is not accepted
C) The debt is invalid because it is a gift
D) The debt binds the partner only if the wife corroborates

Key Takeaway
Admission binds both partners except when made to someone whose testimony is rejected for the admitter.

Admission to Divorced Woman and Umm Walad
Ruling on admission to divorced woman and umm walad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings for admission to a divorced woman versus an umm walad, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf based on testimony admissibility.

Core Idea
Admission to an irrevocably divorced woman is invalid according to Abu Hanifa, but valid for an umm walad due to testimony rules.

This lesson examines two special cases: admission to a woman in her irrevocable divorce waiting period (mu'taddah mubanah) and admission to an emancipated umm walad. The difference in ruling hinges on whether the admitter's testimony would be accepted for that woman. Abu Hanifa and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad differ on the validity of such admissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage discusses admission to an irrevocably divorced woman (mu'taddah mubanah) and to an umm walad who has been freed.
The rulings differ based on testimony acceptance.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa rules that admission to the divorced woman is invalid, because his testimony for her is not accepted.
3
State al-Hasan's narration
Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrates that it is valid, based on a different view that testimony for such a woman is accepted.
This is a weaker narration.
4
Explain the umm walad case
If he frees his umm walad and then admits a debt to her, it binds both partners, even if she is in her waiting period, because his testimony for her is accepted.
This contrasts with the divorced woman.

يلزم شريكه أيضاً إلا لعبده ومكاتبه. ولو أقر المعتدته المبانة لم يصح عند أبي حنيفة، وروى الحسن أنه يصح بناء على أنه لا تقبل شهادته لمعتدته في ظاهر الرواية، وفي رواية الحسن تقبل ولو أعتق أم ولده ثم أقر لها بدين يلزمهما وإن كانت في عدته بخلاف المبانة المعتدة والفرق أن شهادته لأم ولده المعتقة جائزة بخلاف المعتدة عن نكاح وتمامه في المحيط . وإذا باع أحد المتفاوضين من صاحبه ثوباً من شريكه ليقطعه قميصاً لنفسه جاز بخلاف ما إذا باع أحدهما من صاحبه شيئاً من الشركة لأجل التجارة حيث لا يجوز، وكذلك لو باع جارية ليطأها أو طعاماً ليجعله رزقاً لأهله جاز البيع؛ كذا في الظهيرية. وهذا يستثنى من قوله ما لزم قوله : (احترازاً عن أرشي الجنايات على بني آدم قال في النهر أما الجناية على الدابة أو الثوب فتلزمه في قول الإمام ومحمد لما يخفى. وأشار المصنف بلزوم الأنواع الثلاثة إلى أن الدعوى إذا وقعت على أحدهما فأراد المدعي استحلاف الآخر فإن له ذلك. قال الولوالجي في فتاواه: لو ادعى على أحد المتفاوضين فجحد فاستحلف فأراد المدعي استحلاف الآخر فإن القاضي يستحلفه على علمه لأن الدعوى على أحدهما دعوى عليهما، ولو ادعى عليهما شيئاً كان له أن يستحلف كل واحد منهما ألبتة لأن كل واحد منهما يستحلف على فعل نفسه فأيهما نكل عن اليمين يمضي الأمر عليهما لأن إقرار أحدهما كإقرارهما، ولو ادعى على أحدهما وهو غائب كان له أن يستحلف الحاضر على علمه لأنه فعل غيره، فإن حلف ثم قدم الغائب كان له أن يستحلفه ألبتة لأنه يستحلفه على فعل نفسه. ولو ادعى رجل على أحد المتفاوضين جراحة خطأ لها أرش واستحلفه ألبتة فحلف ثم أراد أن يستحلف خاصة إذا باعه لم يكن لشريكه أن يطالب بالثمن ولا للمشتري أن يطالب الشريك بتسليم المبيع.

أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master and becomes free upon his death.

Sale Between Partners: Valid vs. Invalid
Ruling on sale between Mufawada partners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid and invalid sales between Mufawada partners, and explain why valid sales create personal liability, not partnership liability.

Core Idea
A sale between partners is valid for personal use but invalid for trade, and the buyer's liability is personal.

Can a Mufawada partner sell partnership property to his partner? The answer depends on the purpose. If the sale is for personal consumption, it is valid and the buyer alone is liable for the price. But if the sale is for resale as part of trade, it is invalid because the transaction would lack true exchange. This lesson explores that distinction and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The author contrasts a sale for personal use (e.g., to make a shirt) with a sale for trade purposes.
2
State the ruling for personal use
Such a sale is valid, and the buyer alone is liable for the price; the partner is not liable.
This is an exception to the rule that what binds one through trade binds both.
3
State the ruling for trade purposes
Such a sale is invalid because it would involve buying and selling within the same partnership without real change of ownership.
4
Apply the exception
The passage notes that if the sale is for personal use, the buyer's debt is personal; it does not bind the seller's partner.
This is a clear exception to the general rule.

Source Text

وكل شيء ثبت لأحدهما بتجارة ونحوها فللآخر قبضه والمطالبة به لكان أفود لما في الظهيرية : فإن باع أحد المتفاوضين أو أدان رجلاً أو كفل له رجل بدين أو غصب مالاً فلشريكه الآخر أن يطالب وكل شيء هو لأحدهما خاصة إذا باعه لم يكن لشريكه أن يطالب بالثمن ولا للمشتري أن يطالب الشريك بتسليم المبيع.
He [the author] made the binding of the three things absolute, so it includes when an obligation becomes due on one of them through his admission, for it is upon both, because he informed about a matter that he is capable of initiating. Thus in al-Muhit, except if he admits to someone whose testimony is not accepted for him, then it binds only him, like his parents, children, and wife. According to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], it also binds his partner, except for his slave and his mukatab. If he admits to his divorced wife who is irrevocably divorced, it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. Al-Hasan narrated that it is valid, based on the fact that his testimony for his divorced woman is not accepted in the apparent narration, and in al-Hasan's narration it is accepted. If he emancipates his umm walad and then admits a debt to her, it binds both, even if she is in her waiting period, unlike the irrevocably divorced woman in her waiting period. The difference is that his testimony for his emancipated umm walad is permissible, unlike for a woman divorced from marriage. Its completion is in al-Muhit. If one of the two partners sells a garment to his partner from [the stock of] his partner to cut it into a shirt for himself, it is permissible, unlike if one of them sells to his partner something from the partnership for the purpose of trade, for it is not permissible. Similarly, if he sells a slave-girl to have intercourse with her, or food to make it provisions for his family, the sale is permissible. Thus in al-Zahiriyya. This is an exception to his statement: 'What becomes binding on one through trade binds the other,' for the buyer from his partner in the permissible sale scenario is liable for the price, but his partner is not liable. So it is said: except when the creditor is the partner, just as partnership is not valid except with the word mufawada so that the word is evidence of the meaning of generality. End. His statement: 'As a precaution against the blood money for injuries on humans.' He said in al-Nahr: As for injury to an animal or garment, it binds him according to Imam [Abu Hanifa] and Muhammad, as is obvious. And the author indicated by the binding of the three types that if a claim is made against one of them, and the claimant wants to administer an oath to the other, he may do so. Al-Walwaliji said in his Fatawa: If someone claims against one of the two partners and he denies, so he is sworn, and then the claimant wants to swear the other, the judge swears him on his knowledge, because the claim against one is a claim against both. If he claims something against both, he may swear each of them absolutely, because each is sworn concerning his own act. Whichever of them refuses the oath, the matter is finalized against both, because the admission of one is like the admission of both. If he claims against one while he is absent, he may swear the present one on his knowledge, because it is the act of another. If he swears and then the absent one arrives, he may swear him absolutely, because he swears him concerning his own act. If a man claims against one of the two partners for a wound by mistake which has blood money, and swears him absolutely, and he swears, then he wants to swear his partner, he may not do so. Similarly for dower, khul', and compromise for deliberate bloodshed, because these things are not included under the partnership, so the act of one is not like the act of both. End. And his statement 'by trade' includes the dower of a purchased slave-girl who is cohabited with if she is recovered. He said in al-Zahiriyya: If one of the two partners cohabits with a purchased slave-girl and then the slave-girl is recovered, the rightful owner may take whichever he wishes for the 'uqr [indemnity], and this is not like the dower in marriage, because the 'uqr here became obligatory due to trade, unlike the dower. End. If the author had said after this general rule: 'And everything established for one of them through trade or the like, the other may take possession of it and demand it,' it would be more beneficial, due to what is in al-Zahiriyya: If one of the two partners sells a slave or gives a loan to a man, or a man gives him a surety for a debt, or he usurps property, his partner may demand [it]. But if something belongs exclusively to one of them, and he sells it, his partner may not demand the price, nor may the buyer demand delivery from the partner.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage expands on the earlier ruling about the three obligations that bind both partners. It clarifies that an admission (iqrar) by one partner concerning a trade debt binds both partners, because he has informed about a matter he is capable of initiating (i.e., he could have created the obligation himself). However, an exception is made if the admission is made to someone whose testimony would not be accepted for him, such as his ascendants, descendants, or wife—in that case, it binds only the admitting partner. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), it also binds the partner except in the case of the admitting partner's slave or mukatab. A further divergence is noted regarding admission to a divorced woman during her waiting period: Abu Hanifa does not validate it, while al-Hasan ibn Ziyad's narration holds it valid based on differing views on the admissibility of testimony for such a woman. The passage then discusses a sale between partners: if one partner sells a garment from the partnership stock to the other partner for the latter's personal use (e.g., to make a shirt), the sale is valid because it is not a trade transaction. But if the sale is for resale as part of trade, it is invalid because it would involve buying and selling within the same partnership without real change of ownership. Similarly, selling a slave-girl for intercourse or food for personal consumption is valid. This is an exception to the general rule that what binds one through trade binds the other—here, the buyer is personally liable for the price, but the seller's partner is not. The passage then discusses procedural aspects: if a claim is made against one partner, the claimant may swear the other partner on his knowledge because the claim is effectively against both. But if the claim is for something not arising from trade (like blood money, dower, etc.), the other partner cannot be sworn because those matters are not part of the partnership. Finally, the passage notes that if a purchased slave-girl is cohabited with and then found to belong to another, the 'uqr (indemnity) binds both partners because it arose from trade (the purchase), unlike dower in marriage. The commentator suggests that the author could have articulated a more comprehensive rule: everything that becomes due to one partner through trade or similar transactions, the other may also claim and take possession. Examples include sale, loan, surety, and usurpation—all bind both. But if the property belonged exclusively to one partner, the other has no right to the price or delivery.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): A form of partnership with full mutual agency in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Mufawada partner sells a box of dates from the partnership to his partner for his family's personal consumption. Is the sale valid?
A) Yes, because it is for personal use
B) No, because it is a sale between partners
C) Yes, because dates are food
D) No, because it requires the partner's approval

Key Takeaway
Sales between Mufawada partners for personal use are valid and create personal liability, not partnership liability.

Shirkah — Partnership
intermediate

Procedural Rulings and Other Obligations
Procedural rules for oaths and scope of partnership obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules for swearing oaths in claims against one partner and identify which obligations (like blood money, dower, 'uqr) are covered by the partnership.

Core Idea
Claims against one partner are claims against both, but only trade-related obligations bind the partnership.

The final section of the passage discusses procedural matters: when a claimant can swear the partner of the defendant, and which types of claims affect the partnership. It also clarifies that the 'uqr (indemnity) for cohabiting with a purchased slave-girl binds both because it arises from trade, unlike dower in marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the procedural rules
The text states that if a claim is made against one partner, the claimant may also swear the other partner on his knowledge, because the claim is effectively against both.
This is because the admission of one is like the admission of both.
2
List exceptions where the partner cannot be sworn
For claims involving blood money for unintentional wounds, dower, khul', and compromise for deliberate killing, the partner cannot be sworn because these are not partnership matters.
These are personal to the partner.
3
Analyze the 'uqr case
If one partner cohabits with a purchased slave-girl and she is later found to belong to another, the 'uqr (indemnity) binds both because it arose from the trade (the purchase).
This contrasts with dower (mahr) in marriage, which is not trade-related.
4
Generalize the rule
The author suggests that anything established through trade or its like can be claimed by or from the partner; but if something belongs exclusively to one partner, the other has no claim.

Source Text

قوله : (احترازاً عن أرشي الجنايات على بني آدم قال في النهر أما الجناية على الدابة أو الثوب فتلزمه في قول الإمام ومحمد لما يخفى. وأشار المصنف بلزوم الأنواع الثلاثة إلى أن الدعوى إذا وقعت على أحدهما فأراد المدعي استحلاف الآخر فإن له ذلك. قال الولوالجي في فتاواه: لو ادعى على أحد المتفاوضين فجحد فاستحلف فأراد المدعي استحلاف الآخر فإن القاضي يستحلفه على علمه لأن الدعوى على أحدهما دعوى عليهما، ولو ادعى عليهما شيئاً كان له أن يستحلف كل واحد منهما ألبتة لأن كل واحد منهما يستحلف على فعل نفسه فأيهما نكل عن اليمين يمضي الأمر عليهما لأن إقرار أحدهما كإقرارهما، ولو ادعى على أحدهما وهو غائب كان له أن يستحلف الحاضر على علمه لأنه فعل غيره، فإن حلف ثم قدم الغائب كان له أن يستحلفه ألبتة لأنه يستحلفه على فعل نفسه. ولو ادعى رجل على أحد المتفاوضين جراحة خطأ لها أرش واستحلفه ألبتة فحلف ثم أراد أن يستحلف شريكه لم يكن له ذلك، وكذلك المهر والخلع والصلح عن دم العمد لأن هذه الأشياء غير داخلة تحت الشركة فلا يكون فعل أحدهما كفعلهما .اهـ. وشمل قوله بتجارة مهر المشتراة الموطوءة إذا استحقت. قال في الظهيرية : خاصة إذا باعه لم يكن لشريكه أن يطالب بالثمن ولا للمشتري أن يطالب الشريك بتسليم المبيع.

Shirkah — Partnership
intermediate
8 min
Invalidation of Mufawada by Gift or Inheritance
Ruling with proof: invalidation of Mufawada by gift or inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that Mufawada becomes invalid when one partner receives a gift or inheritance of capital-eligible property, and explain why it transforms into an 'Inan partnership.

Core Idea
A gift or inheritance to one partner breaks Mufawada's equality, but the contract survives as an 'Inan partnership.

This lesson covers a key condition of Mufawada: complete equality in capital. When one partner acquires additional property through a gift or inheritance, equality is lost. However, the contract does not become void; it converts to an 'Inan partnership, which does not require equality. We will explore the reasoning and the exact conditions for this transformation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalidating events
The passage states that Mufawada becomes invalid (batil) if one partner is given a gift or inherits property that can serve as partnership capital. Note that charity and bequest are analogous.
Gift must be with possession (qabd) to count.
2
Understand the reason: loss of equality
Equality (musawah) in capital is a condition for Mufawada at inception and throughout. The other partner does not share in the gift/inheritance because there is no cause (sabab) for sharing—it is not from trade.
This is the core reason for invalidity.
3
Recognize the consequence: conversion to 'Inan
The contract does not become null; it turns into an 'Inan partnership. 'Inan does not require equality, and the contract is not binding (ghayr lazim), so it can continue in modified form.
This is a practical mercy—the partnership is not completely lost.
4
Apply to analogous cases
Charity (sadaqah) and bequest (wasiyyah) are treated like gifts. Also, the property must be of a type that partnership is valid in—silver/gold dirhams and current fulus.
Non-capital items (e.g., consumables) may not trigger this rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطلت إن وهب لأحدهما أو ورث ما تصح فيه الشركة أي المفاوضة لفوات المساواة فيما يصلح رأس المال إذ هي شرط فيه ابتداء، وبقاء، وهذا لأن الآخر لا يشاركه فيما أصابه لانعدام السبب في حقه إلا أنها تنقلب عناناً للإمكان فإن المساواة ليست شرطاً فيها ولدوامه حكم الابتداء لكونه غير لازم، وسيأتي أن ما تصح فيه الدراهم والدنانير والفلوس النافقة. وأراد بالهبة الهبة مع القبض والصدقة كالهبة وكذا الوصية، وكذا لو زادت قيمة دراهم أحدهما البيض على دراهم الآخر السود أو دنانيره قبل الشراء. قيد بالزيادة في القدر احترازاً عن الزيادة في القيمة فإنها على ثلاثة أوجه : فإن حصل الفضل قبل الشراء بالمالين فسدت، وإن حصل الفضل بعد الشراء بالمالين وبعد التسليم إلى البائع لا تفسد المفاوضة وإن حصل بعد الشراء بالمالين وقبل التسليم إلى البائع لا تفسد استحساناً. وإن حصل الشراء بأحد المالين ثم فضل أحد المالين فإن فضل المال الذي حصل به الشراء لا تفسد المفاوضة أنه يملك المجني عليه بالضمان؛ قاله الحدادي قوله : (يستحلف كل واحد ألبتة) أي اليمين البتة
And it [Mufawada] becomes invalid if one of them is given a gift or inherits something in which partnership is valid, i.e., Mufawada, because equality is lost in what can serve as capital, since it is a condition initially and ongoing. And this is because the other does not share in what came to him due to the absence of a cause. Rather, it becomes an 'Inan partnership, because equality is not a condition in it, and its continuation has the ruling of initiation because it is not binding. It will come that what partnership is valid in is silver and gold dirhams and current fulus. By gift he means gift with possession, and charity is like a gift, and similarly a bequest. Also, if the value of one's white dirhams exceeds the other's black dirhams, or his dinars, before purchase. He restricted it to increase in quantity as a precaution against increase in value, for it is of three types: if the surplus occurs before purchase with the two capitals, it becomes invalid; if the surplus occurs after purchase with the two capitals and after delivery to the seller, it does not invalidate Mufawada; if it occurs after purchase with the two capitals and before delivery to the seller, it does not invalidate by istihsan. If the purchase was made with one of the two capitals and then one of the capitals becomes superior, then if the superior capital is the one with which the purchase was made, it does not invalidate Mufawada because he owns the victim by guarantee; said by al-Haddadi. His statement: (He swears each one absolutely) i.e., the definite oath.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions that invalidate a Mufawada partnership. The base ruling is that Mufawada becomes invalid (batil) if one partner receives a gift or inheritance consisting of property that could serve as capital (gold, silver dirhams, or current fulus). The reason is that Mufawada requires complete equality (musawah) in capital, both at inception and throughout its duration. If one partner acquires additional property through gift or inheritance, the other does not share in it because there is no cause (sabab) for sharing (the gift/inheritance is not from trade). However, the contract does not become completely void; it transforms into an 'Inan partnership (a limited partnership where equality is not required), because 'Inan does not require equality and the contract is not binding (lazim) so it can continue in a modified form. The commentator specifies that 'gift' means a gift with possession (qabd), and charity (sadaqah) and bequest (wasiyyah) are analogous. Additionally, if before any purchase, the value of one partner's coins (e.g., white dirhams vs. black dirhams) becomes greater than the other's, equality is lost and Mufawada becomes invalid. The increase in value (ziyadah fi al-qimah) is distinguished from increase in quantity; the ruling on value increase depends on timing: (1) if the surplus occurs before any purchase with the joint capital, Mufawada becomes invalid; (2) if it occurs after purchase and after delivery to the seller, it does not invalidate; (3) if it occurs after purchase but before delivery to the seller, it does not invalidate by istihsan (juristic preference). If the purchase was made with only one partner's capital, and that capital later increases, it does not invalidate Mufawada. The final sentence about 'he swears each one absolutely' appears to be a marginal note or a fragment from a different section, possibly regarding oaths in claims against partners.

Key Terms
عنان (ʿinān): A type of partnership where partners contribute capital but do not have full agency for each other; equality is not required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to a Mufawada partnership if one partner receives a gift of 100 silver dirhams?
A) The partnership becomes void
B) The partnership remains valid
C) The partnership becomes an 'Inan partnership
D) The gift is shared equally

Key Takeaway
Mufawada requires equality in capital; a gift or inheritance of capital-eligible property to one partner invalidates it but converts it to an 'Inan partnership.

Increase in Value of Mufawada Capital: Three Scenarios
Ruling with proof: effect of increase in coin value on Mufawada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between increase in quantity and increase in value, and apply the three timing scenarios to determine when an increase in value invalidates Mufawada.

Core Idea
Increase in value before purchase invalidates Mufawada; after purchase does not.

This lesson addresses a subtle case: when the value of one partner's coins increases relative to the other's, does it invalidate Mufawada? The ruling depends on timing – whether the increase occurs before or after purchase with the joint capital. We will also consider a special case when purchase is made with only one partner's capital.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between increase in quantity and value
The author restricts the rule to increase in quantity (ziyada fi al-qadr) as a precaution. Increase in value (ziyada fi al-qimah) is treated differently, with multiple scenarios.
Quantity increase (e.g., one has white dirhams, other black) directly breaks equality; value increase is assessed by timing.
2
Understand the three scenarios for value increase
The passage lists three situations: (1) Surplus before purchase with both capitals – invalidates. (2) After purchase and delivery to seller – does not invalidate. (3) After purchase but before delivery – does not invalidate by istihsan (juristic preference).
Istihsan is applied to preserve the contract when the purchase is not yet complete.
3
Apply the special case: purchase with one capital
If the purchase was made with only one partner's capital, and that capital later increases, it does not invalidate Mufawada because the purchaser owns the acquired item by guarantee (damān).
This is an exception to the general rule.
4
Summarize the principle
The overarching principle is that equality is required only for the capital itself, not for gains from trade or post-purchase fluctuations. The critical moment is before purchase.
This balances the strict condition of equality with the practical needs of ongoing business.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطلت إن وهب لأحدهما أو ورث ما تصح فيه الشركة أي المفاوضة لفوات المساواة فيما يصلح رأس المال إذ هي شرط فيه ابتداء، وبقاء، وهذا لأن الآخر لا يشاركه فيما أصابه لانعدام السبب في حقه إلا أنها تنقلب عناناً للإمكان فإن المساواة ليست شرطاً فيها ولدوامه حكم الابتداء لكونه غير لازم، وسيأتي أن ما تصح فيه الدراهم والدنانير والفلوس النافقة. وأراد بالهبة الهبة مع القبض والصدقة كالهبة وكذا الوصية، وكذا لو زادت قيمة دراهم أحدهما البيض على دراهم الآخر السود أو دنانيره قبل الشراء. قيد بالزيادة في القدر احترازاً عن الزيادة في القيمة فإنها على ثلاثة أوجه : فإن حصل الفضل قبل الشراء بالمالين فسدت، وإن حصل الفضل بعد الشراء بالمالين وبعد التسليم إلى البائع لا تفسد المفاوضة وإن حصل بعد الشراء بالمالين وقبل التسليم إلى البائع لا تفسد استحساناً. وإن حصل الشراء بأحد المالين ثم فضل أحد المالين فإن فضل المال الذي حصل به الشراء لا تفسد المفاوضة أنه يملك المجني عليه بالضمان؛ قاله الحدادي قوله : (يستحلف كل واحد ألبتة) أي اليمين البتة
And it [Mufawada] becomes invalid if one of them is given a gift or inherits something in which partnership is valid, i.e., Mufawada, because equality is lost in what can serve as capital, since it is a condition initially and ongoing. And this is because the other does not share in what came to him due to the absence of a cause. Rather, it becomes an 'Inan partnership, because equality is not a condition in it, and its continuation has the ruling of initiation because it is not binding. It will come that what partnership is valid in is silver and gold dirhams and current fulus. By gift he means gift with possession, and charity is like a gift, and similarly a bequest. Also, if the value of one's white dirhams exceeds the other's black dirhams, or his dinars, before purchase. He restricted it to increase in quantity as a precaution against increase in value, for it is of three types: if the surplus occurs before purchase with the two capitals, it becomes invalid; if the surplus occurs after purchase with the two capitals and after delivery to the seller, it does not invalidate Mufawada; if it occurs after purchase with the two capitals and before delivery to the seller, it does not invalidate by istihsan. If the purchase was made with one of the two capitals and then one of the capitals becomes superior, then if the superior capital is the one with which the purchase was made, it does not invalidate Mufawada because he owns the victim by guarantee; said by al-Haddadi. Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions that invalidate a Mufawada partnership. The base ruling is that Mufawada becomes invalid (batil) if one partner receives a gift or inheritance consisting of property that could serve as capital (gold, silver dirhams, or current fulus). The reason is that Mufawada requires complete equality (musawah) in capital, both at inception and throughout its duration. If one partner acquires additional property through gift or inheritance, the other does not share in it because there is no cause (sabab) for sharing (the gift/inheritance is not from trade). However, the contract does not become completely void; it transforms into an 'Inan partnership (a limited partnership where equality is not required), because 'Inan does not require equality and the contract is not binding (lazim) so it can continue in a modified form. The commentator specifies that 'gift' means a gift with possession (qabd), and charity (sadaqah) and bequest (wasiyyah) are analogous. Additionally, if before any purchase, the value of one partner's coins (e.g., white dirhams vs. black dirhams) becomes greater than the other's, equality is lost and Mufawada becomes invalid. The increase in value (ziyadah fi al-qimah) is distinguished from increase in quantity; the ruling on value increase depends on timing: (1) if the surplus occurs before any purchase with the joint capital, Mufawada becomes invalid; (2) if it occurs after purchase and after delivery to the seller, it does not invalidate; (3) if it occurs after purchase but before delivery to the seller, it does not invalidate by istihsan (juristic preference). If the purchase was made with only one partner's capital, and that capital later increases, it does not invalidate Mufawada. The final sentence about 'he swears each one absolutely' appears to be a marginal note or a fragment from a different section, possibly regarding oaths in claims against partners.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a method of legal reasoning that deviates from strict qiyas for a more equitable result.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It becomes invalid
B) It remains valid
C) It becomes an 'Inan partnership
D) It is void from the start

Key Takeaway
Increase in value of one partner's capital only invalidates Mufawada if it occurs before any purchase with the joint capital; after purchase it is ignored.

Shirkah — Partnership

When Mufawadha Partnership Is Voided: Gift, Inheritance, and Equality
Ruling statement on effect of gift/inheritance and litigation rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of gift and inheritance on mufawadha partnership, identify the condition of equality of capital, and apply the litigation rules for oaths in partnership disputes.

Core Idea
A gift or inheritance voids mufawadha because equality of capital is lost; it then transforms into an 'inan partnership.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling from the Hanafi school on mufawadha partnership. While acts of trade, forcible seizure, and guarantee bind both partners, gifts and inheritances received by one partner break the essential condition of capital equality, voiding the partnership. We also explore the procedural implications for lawsuits, including the rules for administering oaths when a claim is made against one or both partners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts that bind the other partner
Determine which acquisitions by one partner make the other liable. According to the text, trade, forcible seizure (ghasb), and guarantee (kafala) bind the other partner because they fall within the scope of the mufawadha partnership.
These are contrasted with gifts and inheritances, which do not bind the other partner.
2
Recognize when equality of capital is required
Understand that mufawadha requires equality of capital (e.g., same amount of dirhams, dinars, or fulus) both at inception and continuously. If one partner receives a gift or inheritance, equality is lost, voiding the mufawadha.
Equality is not required for 'inan partnership, which is why the voided mufawadha may become 'inan.
3
Understand the transformation to 'inan partnership
When mufawadha is voided due to loss of equality, it does not automatically dissolve entirely; it transforms into an 'inan partnership if possible. This is because 'inan does not require equality of capital.
This preserves the partnership relationship, albeit with reduced authority.
4
Apply oath rules for claims against one partner
Learn the procedural rules for litigation: If a claim is made against one partner, the plaintiff may also have the other partner take an oath based on knowledge. If the claim is against both, each must take an oath about his own act, and refusal by one affects both.
Special rules apply for absent partners and for claims involving personal liabilities like dower or arsh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بتجارة وغصب وكفالة لزم الآخر وبطلت إن وهب لأحدهما أو ورث ما تصح فيه يخفى. وأشار المصنف بلزوم الأنواع الثلاثة إلى أن الدعوى إذا وقعت على أحدهما فأراد المدعي استحلاف الآخر فإن له ذلك. قال الولوالجي في فتاواه: لو ادعى على أحد المتفاوضين فجحد فاستحلف فأراد المدعي استحلاف الآخر فإن القاضي يستحلفه على علمه لأن الدعوى على أحدهما دعوى عليهما، ولو ادعى عليهما شيئاً كان له أن يستحلف كل واحد منهما ألبتة لأن كل واحد منهما يستحلف على فعل نفسه فأيهما نكل عن اليمين يمضي الأمر عليهما لأن إقرار أحدهما كإقرارهما، ولو ادعى على أحدهما وهو غائب كان له أن يستحلف الحاضر على علمه لأنه فعل غيره، فإن حلف ثم قدم الغائب كان له أن يستحلفه ألبتة لأنه يستحلفه على فعل نفسه. ولو ادعى رجل على أحد المتفاوضين جراحة خطأ لها أرش واستحلفه ألبتة فحلف ثم أراد أن يستحلف شريكه لم يكن له ذلك، وكذلك المهر والخلع والصلح عن دم العمد لأن هذه الأشياء غير داخلة تحت الشركة فلا يكون فعل أحدهما كفعلهما .اهـ. وشمل قوله بتجارة مهر المشتراة الموطوءة إذا استحقت. قال في الظهيرية : وإذا وطئ أحد المتفاوضين الجارية المشتراة ثم استحقت الجارية فللمستحق أن يأخذ أيهما شاء بالعقر وليس ذلك كالمهر في النكاح لأن العقر ههنا وجب بسبب التجارة بخلاف المهر اهـ. ولو قال المصنف بعد هذه الكلية وكل شيء ثبت لأحدهما بتجارة ونحوها فللآخر قبضه والمطالبة به لكان أفود لما في الظهيرية : فإن باع أحد المتفاوضين أو أدان رجلاً أو كفل له رجل بدين أو غصب مالاً فلشريكه الآخر أن يطالب وكل شيء هو لأحدهما خاصة إذا باعه لم يكن لشريكه أن يطالب بالثمن ولا للمشتري أن يطالب الشريك بتسليم المبيع.
قوله: (وبطلت أن وهب لأحدهما أو ورث ما تصح فيه الشركة) أي المفاوضة لفوات المساواة فيما يصلح رأس المال إذ هي شرط فيه ابتداء ،وبقاء، وهذا لأن الآخر لا يشاركه فيما أصابه لانعدام السبب في حقه إلا أنها تنقلب عناناً للإمكان فإن المساواة ليست شرطاً فيها ولدوامه حكم الابتداء لكونه غير لازم، وسيأتي أن ما تصح فيه الدراهم والدنانير والفلوس النافقة. وأراد بالهبة الهبة مع القبض والصدقة كالهبة وكذا الوصية، وكذا لو زادت قيمة دراهم أحدهما البيض على دراهم الآخر السود أو دنانيره قبل الشراء. قيد بالزيادة في القدر احترازاً عن الزيادة في القيمة فإنها على ثلاثة أوجه : فإن حصل الفضل قبل الشراء بالمالين فسدت، وإن حصل الفضل بعد الشراء بالمالين وبعد التسليم إلى البائع لا تفسد المفاوضة وإن حصل بعد الشراء بالمالين وقبل التسليم إلى البائع لا تفسد استحساناً. وإن حصل الشراء بأحد المالين ثم فضل أحد المالين فإن فضل المال الذي حصل به الشراء لا تفسد المفاوضة وإن فضل المال الذي لم يحصل به الشراء فسدت والفرق أنه في القدر إنما هو فضل أحدهما صاحبه فيما يصلح رأس مال المفاوضة فإن المشتري بينهما على الشركة ولأحدها زيادة دراهم بخلاف الزيادة من حيث القيمة بعد الشراء فإنها حصلت في مال الغير لا في مال أحدهما فلم يفت التساوي في مالهما ؛ كذا في المحيط قوله : (لا العرض) أي لا تبطل بملك العرض لأنه لا تصح فيه الشركة فلا تشترط المساواة فيه، ولو قال لا ما لا تصح فيه لكان أولى ليدخل العقار والديون فإنها لا تبطل بهما إلا إذا قبض الديون
Book of Partnership. By trade, forcible seizure, and guarantee, the other is bound, and it becomes void if one of them is given a gift or inherits what partnership is valid in, it becomes hidden. And the author indicated by the binding of the three types that if a claim occurs against one of them and the plaintiff wants to have the other take an oath, he has that right. Al-Walwaliji said in his Fatawa: If a claim is made against one of the two partners in mufawadha, and he denies, and the plaintiff asks him to take an oath, and then wants the other partner to take an oath, the judge makes him take an oath based on his knowledge because the claim against one is a claim against both. And if a claim is made against both of them for something, he has the right to have each of them take an absolute oath, because each of them takes an oath concerning his own act; whichever of them refuses the oath, the matter proceeds against both of them, because the acknowledgment of one is like the acknowledgment of both. And if a claim is made against one of them while he is absent, he has the right to have the present partner take an oath based on his knowledge because it is an act of someone else; then if he takes an oath and the absent one later appears, he has the right to have him take an absolute oath because he is taking an oath about his own act. And if a man claims against one of the two partners in mufawadha an unintentional wound that has compensation (arsh), and he has him take an absolute oath, then he wants to have his partner take an oath, he does not have that right. Similarly for the dower (mahr), khul', and reconciliation for intentional homicide, because these things are not included in the partnership, so the act of one is not like the act of both. End quote. And his statement 'by trade' includes the dower of a female slave who was purchased and then used for intercourse if she is reclaimed. It is said in al-Zahiriyya: If one of the two partners in mufawadha uses a purchased slave-girl for intercourse and then she is reclaimed, the rescuer may take whichever he wishes for the compensation for intercourse ('uqr), and this is not like the dower in marriage because the compensation here is incurred due to trade, unlike the dower. End quote. If the author had said after this general principle: 'And every thing established for one of them through trade and the like, the other has the right to receive it and demand it,' it would have been more beneficial, as in al-Zahiriyya: If one of the two partners in mufawadha sells, or lends to a man, or a man guarantees a debt for him, or seizes property by force, his partner has the right to demand, but everything that belongs to one of them specifically, if he sells it, his partner does not have the right to demand the price, nor does the buyer have the right to demand delivery from the partner. His statement: (And it becomes void if one of them is given a gift or inherits what partnership is valid in) — that is, the mufawadha partnership, because equality is lost in what can serve as capital, since it is a condition for it initially and continuously. This is because the other does not share with him in what he acquired due to the absence of the cause in his case, except that it turns into an 'inan partnership because it is possible, since equality is not a condition for it, and its continuity has the ruling of the beginning because it is not binding. It will come that what partnership is valid in are dirhams, dinars, and current fulus. By gift he means gift with possession, and charity is like a gift, and likewise a bequest. Also, if the value of the white dirhams of one of them exceeds the black dirhams of the other, or his dinars before purchase. He restricted it by increase in quantity to avoid increase in value, for it is of three types: if the excess occurs before the purchase with both sums, it becomes invalid; if the excess occurs after the purchase with both sums and after delivery to the seller, it does not invalidate the mufawadha; if it occurs after the purchase with both sums and before delivery to the seller, it does not invalidate by istihsan. If the purchase occurred with one of the two sums and then one of the sums exceeds, if the excess is in the sum with which the purchase was made, it does not invalidate the mufawadha, but if the excess is in the sum with which the purchase was not made, it becomes invalid. The difference is that the increase in quantity is one partner surpassing the other in what is valid as capital for mufawadha, because the purchased item is between them on partnership and one of them has more dirhams, unlike the increase in value after purchase, for it occurs in the property of another, not in the property of one of them, so equality in their property is not lost. As in al-Muhit. His statement: (not 'ard) — that is, it is not invalidated by ownership of a commodity because partnership is not valid in it, so equality is not required in it. If he had said 'not what partnership is not valid in,' it would have been better to include real estate and debts, for they do not invalidate it except when the debts are collected.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq explaining the effects of acquiring property through trade, forcible seizure (ghasb), and guarantee (kafala) in a mufawadha partnership, as well as the effect of gift and inheritance. The author first establishes that if one partner acquires property via trade, ghasb, or kafala, the other partner is bound because these are within the scope of the partnership. However, if one partner receives a gift or inheritance, the mufawadha becomes void (batil) because equality of capital, which is a condition for mufawadha, is lost. The gift must be a properly received gift (with possession), and charity and bequest are treated similarly. The commentary then discusses the consequences for litigation: when a claim is against one partner, the plaintiff may also have the other partner take an oath because the claim against one is effectively against both. The author quotes al-Walwaliji's Fatawa to elaborate on the specifics of oaths in various scenarios, including when one partner is absent. A special case is mentioned: if a partner purchases a slave-girl and uses her for intercourse, and she is later reclaimed, the compensation ('uqr) is considered a trade-related liability and thus falls under the partnership, unlike dower (mahr) in marriage which is personal. The passage also details when an increase in the quantity or value of capital affects the validity of mufawadha. Increases in quantity before any purchase invalidate the partnership, but increases in value after purchase do not, with specific exceptions. Finally, the text clarifies that ownership of commodities ('urud), real estate, and debts does not invalidate the mufawadha because partnership is not valid in those types of property, so equality is not required.

Key Terms
مفاوضة (mufāwaḍah): A full agency partnership where each partner is an agent and guarantor for the other, requiring equality of capital and capacity.
عنان (ʿinān): A limited partnership where partners contribute capital but do not have full agency; equality is not required.
مساواة (musāwāh): Equality of the capital contributed by each partner in a mufawadha partnership; a condition for its validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The partnership continues unchanged.
B) The partnership becomes void because equality of capital is lost.
C) The inherited property becomes joint property.
D) The other partner must also inherit to maintain equality.

Key Takeaway
Equality of capital is a defining condition for mufawadha; its loss voids the partnership but allows transformation into 'inan.
